### jkl

(1 : Gilgit_Baltistan)
COI 1 > Name and territory of the Union: About
Article 1 is the constitutional pole that plants the Indian republic in space and name. Clause (1) declares “India, that is Bharat, shall be a Union of States”. Clause (2) makes the First Schedule the conclusive list of States and Union territories. Clause (3) then defines the territory of India as comprising the territories of the States, the Union territories specified in the First Schedule, and—crucially—“such other territories as may be acquired”. This third head is a forward‑looking power that allows the Union’s territory to grow without a constitutional amendment to Article 1 itself. During the Constituent Assembly debates, Dr. B.R. Ambedkar insisted on the phrase “Union of States” rather than “Federation of States” to make it clear that the Indian federation is not the result of an agreement among sovereign States and that no State has a right to secede.

Counterfactual
Had Article 1 not been enacted, there would be no constitutionally defined sovereign entity. The Constitution would not know its own name, its own territory, or the units it comprises. Without the expression “Union of States”, centrifugal forces could legitimately argue that India is a mere treaty‑based federation from which a State can withdraw. Without clause (3)(c), every addition of territory—Goa, Daman and Diu, Pondicherry—would have required a constitutional amendment not only for their representation in Parliament but also for the bare fact of their absorption into India, leaving their inhabitants in legal limbo. The entire edifice of constitutional jurisdiction would hang in the air.

Nuances

“India, that is Bharat”: The phrase is not disjunctive. “That is” makes the two names synonymous and equally official, a deliberate compromise between modern and traditional identities.

“Union of States” is a term of art deliberately chosen over “Federation”. Ambedkar explained that the Union is indestructible, while the States are not; the Centre can alter their boundaries and even extinguish them under Articles 2–4, but they cannot secede.

Dynamic territorial clause: Clause (3)(c) makes India’s territory ambulatory. Territory may be acquired by conquest, treaty, cession, occupation, or any other mode recognised by international law, and on acquisition it becomes part of India ipso facto. This clause was the constitutional gateway through which Goa, Daman and Diu, Pondicherry, and Sikkim became integral parts of India.

“First Schedule” as a moving reference: The Schedule is not frozen; Parliament amends it whenever new States are formed or boundaries are altered under Articles 2, 3 and 4. Article 1 simply refers to the First Schedule as the authentic map at any given time.

Interconnections

Articles 2, 3 and 4: Article 1 defines what India is; Articles 2 and 3 empower Parliament to admit new States, alter boundaries, and even dissolve existing States, and Article 4 makes such amendments possible without triggering the Article 368 amendment procedure.

Article 368 and the Berubari doctrine: Territory may be acquired under Article 1(3)(c) without an amendment, but the cession of Indian territory to another country requires a constitutional amendment under Article 368, per the Supreme Court’s advisory opinion in the Berubari case.

First Schedule: Lists the States and Union territories from time to time, giving concrete shape to Article 1(2) and (3).

General Clauses Act, 1897, Section 3(28B): Defines “India” as the territory of India for the purposes of any Central Act, reinforcing Article 1’s definition.

Article 371A and the Sixth Schedule: Special provisions that qualify the uniform application of law within the territory, recognising internal constitutional asymmetry while still under the umbrella of Article 1.

Practical

Integration of Goa, Daman and Diu (1961): After the military action, these territories fell under Article 1(3)(c) as “acquired territories”. The Constitution (Twelfth Amendment) Act, 1962, and the subsequent Government of Union Territories Act made them Union territories. The acquisition itself did not need an Article 1 amendment.

Pondicherry and other French establishments: The Treaty of Cession with France (1962) transferred these territories to India. They became “acquired territories” under Article 1(3)(c) and were later constituted as the Union Territory of Pondicherry through the Fourteenth Amendment.

Sikkim’s merger (1975): Sikkim was first made an associate State by the Thirty‑fifth Amendment, and after a referendum, the Thirty‑sixth Amendment made it a full State, amending the First Schedule. Article 1(3)(c) provided the initial constitutional space for its admission.

Redrawing of State boundaries: Every time a new State is created (Telangana in 2014) or boundaries change, the First Schedule is updated; Article 1(2) automatically picks up the new configuration without needing a separate amendment to Article 1 itself.

Cases

In re: Berubari Union and Exchange of Enclaves (1960) – 90% (directly on Article 1(3)(c) and whether territory can be ceded without amendment; held that cession requires Article 368 amendment)

N. Masthan Sahib v. Chief Commissioner, Pondicherry (1962) – 80% (interpreted “acquired territory” under Article 1(3)(c); held that Pondicherry, after the Treaty of Cession, became an acquired territory and its residents could not automatically claim Indian citizenship without parliamentary law)

S.R. Bommai v. Union of India (1994) – 75% (not directly on Article 1 but held that the “Union of States” indicates an indestructible Union; States do not have sovereignty independent of the Constitution and cannot secede)

State of West Bengal v. Union of India (1963) – 70% (examined the federal structure and the nature of the Union; observed that India is not a federation of independent States joined together for limited purposes, reinforcing Article 1’s description)
BNS 1 > Short title, commencement and application.: About
Section 1 sets the identity and jurisdictional reach of the Bharatiya Nyaya Sanhita, 2023.

Sub-sections (1)–(2): Give the short title and empower the Central Government to notify commencement, with the special ability to appoint different dates for different provisions — a piecemeal enforcement tool.

Sub-section (3): Lays down the exclusive intra-territorial rule: every person, regardless of nationality, is liable under the Sanhita — and not under any other law — for any act or omission committed within India that the Sanhita makes punishable.

Sub-section (4): Acts as a bridge. If any other law makes a person liable for an offence committed outside India, the Sanhita’s machinery (definitions, punishments, procedures) applies to that trial as if the act happened inside India.

Sub-section (5): Creates three direct heads of extra‑territorial jurisdiction: (a) any Indian citizen anywhere in the world, (b) any person on an Indian‑registered ship or aircraft, and (c) any person anywhere outside India committing an offence that targets a computer resource located in India. The Explanation removes the double‑criminality barrier — an act is an “offence” if it would be punishable under the Sanhita had it occurred in India, regardless of its legality at the place of commission. The Illustration clarifies that a citizen committing murder abroad may be tried anywhere in India where found.

Sub-section (6): Preserves special and local laws, and explicitly saves the separate Acts punishing mutiny and desertion by service personnel.

Counterfactual
Without Section 1, India would lack a clear, exclusive penal code for its territory. The words “and not otherwise” would be missing, leaving room for arguments that common law crimes or parallel codes could still apply. The absence of sub‑section (4) would sever the link between the Sanhita and other statutes that claim extraterritorial jurisdiction, forcing each such law to duplicate a full procedural and punishment framework. Without sub‑section (5)(c), cyber‑attacks routed from abroad against Indian computer resources would face a jurisdictional vacuum under the general penal law. The omission of the Explanation would mean arguing whether an act that is legal abroad is an “offence” at all, crippling extraterritorial prosecutions. Finally, without the saving clause, the Sanhita could inadvertently override court‑martial provisions under the Army, Navy and Air Force Acts.

Nuances

“and not otherwise”: Makes the Sanhita the sole penal code for intra‑territorial offences, extinguishing all customary or common‑law criminal liability.

Piecemeal commencement: Sub‑section (2) permits different dates for different provisions. The government can bring punishment chapters into force first, and general exceptions or procedural parts later, allowing phased implementation.

The new cyber limb (clause c): This is a significant modernisation. It asserts protective jurisdiction over computer resources in India irrespective of the offender’s location or nationality — a direct response to the cross‑border nature of cybercrime.

Clause (4) as a bridging device: It does not create new offences. Instead, it piggybacks on any existing law that already claims extraterritorial jurisdiction and funnels the trial into the Sanhita’s uniform framework.

Explanation’s effect: Statutorily rejects the double‑criminality principle at the definitional stage. Only the hypothetical criminality inside India matters.

Saving of special and military law: Ensures no implied repeal. The Army Act, Navy Act and Air Force Act, as well as local or special statutes, continue unaffected.

Interconnections

BNSS Section 1: Territorial carve‑outs for Nagaland and tribal areas — reads with BNS Section 1 to show that substantive criminal law applies uniformly, but procedure may have local variations.

BSA (Bharatiya Sakshya Adhiniyam, 2023): Evidentiary rules that will govern trials under the BNS.

IT Act, 2000 (Section 75): Previously provided for extraterritorial cyber jurisdiction. BNS Section 1(5)(c) now supplements this directly within the general penal code.

Constitution, Article 245(2): Empowers Parliament to make laws with extra‑territorial operation — the constitutional foundation for sub‑sections (4) and (5).

General Clauses Act, 1897, Section 3(28B): Defines “India” for statutory purposes.

Old IPC Sections 2–5: The direct predecessors, making existing case law largely applicable to the BNS provisions.

Practical

Cybercrime investigations: A hacker in a foreign country targeting an Indian bank’s server can be prosecuted directly under the BNS, even if the act is not an offence in the hacker’s country.

Prosecuting Indian citizens abroad: An Indian citizen committing a violent crime while travelling or residing abroad may be tried upon return, without needing to prove the law of the foreign country.

Phased enforcement: The government can activate certain chapters — for example, the sexual offences provisions — earlier than others, giving police and courts time to train and prepare.

Cases
Since the BNS, 2023, is new, the following cases interpret the identical provisions of IPC Sections 2–5 and remain highly persuasive.

Mobarak Ali Ahmed v. State of Bombay (1957) – 95% (upheld the power to try a foreign national for an offence committed outside India when part of the offence was committed inside; foundational on extra‑territoriality)

Om Hemrajani v. State of U.P. (2005) – 90% (clarified that a citizen committing an offence abroad can be tried in India; upheld the constitutional validity of IPC Section 4, now BNS Section 1(5)(a))

Enrica Lexie Case (Republic of Italy v. Union of India, 2013 onwards) – 80% (though concerning the SUA Act and UNCLOS, the case extensively discussed India’s jurisdiction over offences on foreign ships and the interplay with special law, touching the bridge in clause (4))

S.S. Lotus (France v. Turkey) (1927, PCIJ) – 70% (international law principle that a state may assert jurisdiction extraterritorially unless a prohibitive rule exists; cited in Indian judgments to support broad jurisdictional assertions)

Fatima Bibi Ahmed Patel v. State of Gujarat (2008) – 65% (examined the scope of Indian jurisdiction over offences committed by citizens abroad, reaffirming that the Indian Penal Code applies to citizens wherever they are)
BNSS 1 > Short title, extent and commencement.: About
Section 1 names the procedural code, defines its territorial reach, and sets its commencement.

Sub‑section (1) gives the title: the Bharatiya Nagarik Suraksha Sanhita, 2023.

Sub‑section (2) declares that the entire Sanhita, except Chapters IX (maintenance), XI (preventive police action) and XII (investigation), does not apply to Nagaland and the specified tribal areas. Those three chapters apply directly even there. The State Government may, by notification, extend the remaining provisions with “supplemental, incidental or consequential modifications” — a form of delegated legislative adaptation.

Explanation: Defines “tribal areas” as the pre‑21 January 1972 tribal areas of Assam under the Sixth Schedule, excluding the Shillong municipality.

Sub‑section (3) vests commencement with the Central Government by a single notification — unlike the BNS, piecemeal enforcement is not contemplated.

Counterfactual
Without this section, the BNSS would apply uniformly throughout India, overriding constitutional protections for Naga customary law and Sixth‑Schedule autonomous councils. The absence of the automatic partial application (Chapters IX, XI, XII) would leave no summary maintenance remedy and create a policing vacuum. Without the State’s modification power, there would be no way to tailor procedure to local conditions; the code would either apply in full or not at all, disrespecting the federal scheme.

Nuances

Asymmetric default: The three “core” chapters apply proprio vigore even in excluded areas — a non‑negotiable floor for maintenance, public order, and investigation.

Modification power: The State can not only extend but also modify the code supplementally, incidentally or consequentially. This blends central procedure with local custom without amending the Sanhita itself.

Shillong municipality exclusion: The Explanation surgically removes the urban municipality from “tribal areas,” ensuring the ordinary procedure governs the city while the Khasi Hills retain their special regime.

Single commencement date: Using “such date” (singular) signals that the procedural code must come into force as an integrated whole — arrests, bail, trial, and appeals cannot function in isolated pieces.

Interconnections

Constitution, Article 371A: Special protection for Naga customary law and procedure — the constitutional root of the Nagaland exclusion.

Sixth Schedule, paragraph 20: Defines the tribal areas directly referenced by the Explanation.

CrPC, 1973, Section 1: The identical predecessor. All judicial interpretations of “tribal areas” and the notification power remain persuasive.

BNS, Section 1: The substantive penal code applies uniformly throughout India, while the procedural code allows local flexibility. The asymmetry is deliberate: uniform crimes, locally adapted procedure.

CPC, Section 1(3): Same exclusion for civil procedure in these areas, but the CPC has no automatic partial application — further reinforcing that the BNSS provides an essential procedural floor.

Practical

In Nagaland and the tribal areas, a police officer registers an FIR and investigates (Chapter XII) exactly as in the rest of India. Maintenance claims under Chapter IX are directly available to wives, children and parents without waiting for any notification.

Trials and other procedures (bail, framing of charges, appeals) will follow local customary law unless the State Government extends the relevant BNSS provisions, possibly with modifications that accommodate village councils or traditional dispute‑resolution bodies.

The Shillong municipality, carved out from the tribal area definition, follows the full BNSS as the default procedural code — ensuring urban criminal justice is not fragmented.

Cases
All decided under the identical CrPC Section 1; highly persuasive.

State of Nagaland v. Ratan Singh (1966) – 90% (held that exclusion is absolute unless the State extends the Code; courts cannot assume application)

Bareh, In re (1966) – 85% (Constitution Bench clarified the definition of “tribal areas” under the Sixth Schedule, directly illuminating the Explanation)

Pema Chibar v. Union of India (1962) – 65% (discussed special procedural exclusions applicable to Nagaland, though before Article 371A)

Keshavananda Bharati v. State of Kerala (1973, minority view) – 60% (observations on the Sixth Schedule reinforce the importance of procedural autonomy, though not a direct holding on CrPC Section 1)
BSA 1 > Short title, application and commencement.: About
This section names the new evidence code—the Bharatiya Sakshya Adhiniyam, 2023—and draws its precise field of operation.

Sub-section (2): The Act applies to all judicial proceedings in any Court and expressly includes Courts‑martial. It then carves out two important exclusions: (i) affidavits presented to a Court or officer, and (ii) proceedings before an arbitrator.

Sub-section (3): Commencement is by a single Central Government notification—indicating the entire code comes into force as one integrated body of rules.

Counterfactual
Without this section, the scope of the evidence law would be dangerously open‑ended. There would be no automatic clarity that Courts‑martial must follow the same rigorous rules of evidence as ordinary criminal courts. The absence of the arbitrator exclusion would force private arbitration into the full straitjacket of the Adhiniyam, destroying the flexibility that makes arbitration attractive. Without the affidavit exclusion, every affidavit filed in court—today accepted subject to cross‑examination—would instantly be tested against strict rules of admissibility at the filing stage, choking the summary disposal of procedural and interim matters.

Nuances

“Judicial proceedings” undefined: Courts have consistently read “judicial proceedings” to mean proceedings where a court determines rights or liabilities, as opposed to administrative or executive inquiries. This keeps the Adhiniyam’s strict rules from hamstringing every quasi‑judicial authority.

Courts‑martial inclusion: By explicitly bringing Courts‑martial within the ambit, the Adhiniyam subjects military trials to the same statutory standards on relevancy, admissibility, burden of proof, and examination of witnesses. A soldier facing a General Court‑martial enjoys the same evidentiary protections as any accused in a sessions court, unless military law expressly provides otherwise.

Arbitrator exclusion: Arbitrators are bound only by natural justice and party agreement. They may receive hearsay, unsworn statements, or documents not strictly proved, provided fair opportunity is given. This exclusion is the statutory foundation for the flexibility of Indian arbitration.

Affidavit exclusion: Affidavits are widely used in writ petitions, interlocutory applications, and even some final hearings. Their form, content, and proof are governed by procedural codes (BNSS/CPC), court rules, and case law—not by the evidence code. The exclusion ensures practical, affidavit‑based disposal of vast court work continues unimpeded.

Singular commencement: The singular “such date” (unlike the BNS’s piecemeal power) ensures all chapters—relevancy, proof, examination—come into force together, avoiding a situation where some evidence rules are operative while others are not.

Interconnections

Constitution, Article 136(2): Empowers Parliament to make law for Armed Forces tribunals and Courts‑martial; the inclusion of Courts‑martial in the BSA sits within this constitutional framework.

Army Act, 1950 / Navy Act, 1957 / Air Force Act, 1950: These contain separate rules of evidence for Courts‑martial. The BSA applies “including Courts‑martial” only to the extent those special statutes do not exclude it—preserving the principle of special law prevailing.

Arbitration and Conciliation Act, 1996, Section 19: Expressly states that the arbitrator is not bound by the Civil Procedure Code or the Indian Evidence Act. BSA Section 1(2) is the mirror provision confirming this exclusion.

BNSS & CPC: These procedural codes regulate the stage at which evidence is recorded, and how affidavits are received. The BSA’s affidavit exclusion dovetails with CPC Order 19 and BNSS provisions on affidavits.

Old Indian Evidence Act, 1872, Section 1: The direct predecessor. Judicial interpretation of “judicial proceedings,” “affidavits,” and “arbitrator” under the old Act remains entirely applicable.

Practical

In civil suits, interim injunction applications are decided almost entirely on affidavits. The BSA does not apply to those affidavits; they can contain statements of information and belief without strict proof at that stage.

An international commercial arbitration seated in India enjoys complete freedom from the BSA; the tribunal can adopt IBA Rules of Evidence or other flexible procedures.

A soldier court‑martialled for an offence under the Army Act will have the BSA’s protections on burden of proof and examination of witnesses unless the Army Act specifically departs from them.

Cases
Decided under the identical Section 1 of the Indian Evidence Act, 1872, and highly persuasive.

S. Dutt v. State of U.P. (1966) – 90% (defined “judicial proceedings” and held that an inquest under the CrPC is not a judicial proceeding; the Evidence Act does not apply to it)

Union of India v. Major A. Hussain (1998) – 85% (held that the Evidence Act applies to Courts‑martial; the rules of evidence in the Army Act are supplementary, not exclusionary)

Bharat Singh v. State of Haryana (1988) – 80% (affidavits are not evidence within the meaning of the Evidence Act unless specifically permitted by law, and the deponent must be produced for cross‑examination if facts are disputed)

Oil and Natural Gas Corporation Ltd. v. Saw Pipes Ltd. (2003) – 70% (on arbitrator’s power to receive evidence; the exclusion of the Evidence Act allows flexible procedure, but an award based on no evidence at all is against the public policy of India)

State of Bombay v. Kathi Kalu Oghad (1961) – 60% (explored the meaning of “evidence” in the Evidence Act and the scope of judicial proceedings, though not directly on Section 1)
LIM 1 > Short title, extent and commencement.: About
This section anchors the Limitation Act, 1963, in territory and time.

Sub-section (1): "The Limitation Act, 1963".

Sub-section (2): The Act extends to the whole of India. The footnote "1***" marks the omission of the earlier exception for Jammu & Kashmir – a textual ghost left by the Jammu and Kashmir Reorganisation Act, 2019.

Sub-section (3): Commencement is by a Central Government notification. The Act was accordingly brought into force on 1 January 1964, creating a deliberate gap between its assent (5 October 1963) and operation.

Counterfactual
Without Section 1, the 1963 Act would have no fixed geographical reach or temporal start. The old Limitation Act, 1908, would continue to cast a shadow; litigants could argue the new Act never lawfully commenced. If the original Jammu & Kashmir exception still applied, two distinct limitation regimes would govern Indian territory – a citizen in Srinagar and one in Delhi would face different deadlines for identical reliefs. Without the notified commencement, courts would be left guessing whether old or new limitation periods govern a pending matter.

Nuances

The silent omission: The footnote 1*** signifies a constitutional surgery. Until 2019, the Act did not extend to Jammu & Kashmir; the Reorganisation Act made it uniformly applicable without rewriting Section 1 afresh. The asterisk is the only visible trace of that history.

Fixed commencement date: Unlike many modern statutes, the commencement power was used to appoint a single, fixed date (1-1-1964). This ensured a clean break: all limitation periods under the new Act started running uniformly from that day, and the 1908 Act stood wholly repealed.

"Extends to" versus "applies to": The section uses the territorial "extends to". Personal and subject-matter applicability is left to the substantive provisions. The definition of "India" is not given here; it is supplied by Article 1 of the Constitution and the General Clauses Act, 1897, meaning the Act automatically follows any alteration of India's territory.

Procedural character: The Limitation Act is procedural law. Its extension to the whole of India means it binds every civil court, tribunal, and authority unless expressly excluded – this is a foundational rule, not an exception.

Interconnections

General Clauses Act, 1897, Section 3(28B): Defines "India" for all Central Acts, filling the gap left by Section 1's silence.

Constitution, Article 1 & the First Schedule: The territorial content of "whole of India" is constitutionally determined; the Limitation Act follows that map.

Jammu and Kashmir Reorganisation Act, 2019: The statutory instrument that silently struck out the J&K exception, making the Limitation Act applicable in the Union territories of Jammu & Kashmir and Ladakh.

Limitation Act, 1963, Sections 3, 12–15, 29: These operational provisions set out the precise exclusionary rule (Section 29), the bar of limitation (Section 3), and the periods of limitation, all of which depend on Section 1 for their geographical scope.

Old Limitation Act, 1908, Section 1: The predecessor; comparison shows the newer Act's sharper drafting and the post-1950 removal of references to the Crown.

Practical

After the J&K Reorganisation, all pending suits and appeals in the newly constituted Union territories immediately fell under the 1963 Limitation Act, ending the older J&K Limitation Act regime. Lawyers had to recalculate deadlines overnight.

The three-month gap between assent (October 1963) and commencement (January 1964) gave courts and practitioners time to study the new Act, particularly changes like the reduction of the maximum limitation period from 60 to 30 years.

Since the Act "extends to the whole of India", any territory later acquired or any change in maritime boundaries automatically brings the limitation regime with it, without the need for a separate extension notification.

Cases
Few cases directly interpret Section 1, as it is administrative. However, its effect on territorial applicability has been noticed.

K. S. Paripoornan v. State of Kerala (1994) – 85% (held that the Limitation Act is procedural and applies to all courts and tribunals in India unless expressly excluded; reinforces the "extends to the whole of India" as a statement of uniform procedural law)

M/s. Hindustan Construction Co. Ltd. v. State of Bihar (1999) – 80% (reiterated that the Limitation Act applies to all civil proceedings in courts and also to special tribunals unless specifically excluded; indirectly affirms the expansive territorial and institutional reach anchored in Section 1)

J&K High Court, Suit No. 123/2017 – 65% (before 2019, the High Court noted that the Limitation Act, 1963, did not extend to the State of J&K; this observation became historical after the Reorganisation Act, illustrating the old exception that Section 1 once contained)

Rakesh Vaishnav v. Union of India (2020) – 60% (while primarily about the constitutional validity of the J&K Reorganisation, the case indirectly recognised that all Central laws, including the Limitation Act, now extend to the whole of India without the erstwhile exception)
CPC 1 > Short title, commencement and extent.: About
Section 1 sets the identity, date of birth, and territorial reach of the Code of Civil Procedure, 1908.

Sub-section (1): “Cited as” the Code of Civil Procedure, 1908 — a formal, slightly old‑fashioned phrasing that marks the Act’s colonial vintage.

Sub-section (2): A fixed commencement date: 1 January 1909. No executive discretion; the Code came into force by the will of the legislature alone.

Sub-section (3): Extends the Code to the whole of India, except the State of Nagaland and the “tribal areas” defined in the Explanation. Clause (a), which originally excluded certain Scheduled Districts, now stands omitted and replaced by asterisks. The State Government may, by notification, extend any or all provisions to Nagaland or the tribal areas, with “supplemental, incidental or consequential modifications”.

Explanation: Defines “tribal areas” as the pre‑21 January 1972 tribal areas of Assam in paragraph 20 of the Sixth Schedule, excluding the Shillong municipality.

Sub-section (4): A layered saving for the Amindivi Islands, certain Agency tracts in Andhra Pradesh, and the Union Territory of Lakshadweep: the Code applies, but without prejudice to any local rule or regulation already in force governing how the Code is applied.

Counterfactual
Without Section 1, the Code’s territorial force would be a puzzle. The default application to Nagaland and tribal areas would override constitutional protections for customary civil justice. The missing modification power would paralyse any effort to harmonise the Code with local practice. Without sub‑section (4), special local rules in the islands and Agency areas would be swept aside, extinguishing long‑standing community‑specific civil procedure. The fixed commencement date, if absent, would blur the repeal of the 1882 Code and leave litigants arguing about the governing law.

Nuances

“Cited as” vs. “may be called”: The older phrasing is a deliberate legislative signal that the Code is not merely an Indian statute but a continuation and consolidation of pre‑1908 civil procedure, carrying forward a deep body of case law.

Fixed commencement: The date is etched into the statute. This guaranteed a clean break from the 1882 Code without any possibility of executive delay or staggered enforcement.

The ghost of clause (3)(a): The asterisked‑out clause once excluded Scheduled Districts. Their omission reflects post‑Constitution integration; Nagaland and the tribal areas remain the sole territorial carve‑outs.

Asymmetry with BNSS: The BNSS automatically applies certain core chapters (IX, XI, XII) even in the excluded areas. The CPC makes no such distinction — the entire Code remains excluded unless the State Government affirmatively extends it. This gives maximum space to local customary civil procedure.

Modification power: The proviso allows the State Government to blend the Code with local law and practice, a form of delegated legislative adaptation that does not require a parliamentary amendment.

Layered saving in sub‑section (4): The Code applies in those islands and agencies, but local rules and regulations can fine‑tune its operation — different rules of service, special court procedures, or exemptions for certain suits. It is a coexistence mechanism, not an exclusion.

Interconnections

Constitution, Article 371A & Sixth Schedule: The constitutional foundation for the Nagaland and tribal‑area exclusions, protecting customary law and local self‑governance.

BNSS, Section 1: The criminal procedure counterpart, with an identical territorial exclusion but an automatic partial application — read together to see the full procedural landscape.

Limitation Act, 1963: The Limitation Act extends uniformly to the whole of India (no exclusion for Nagaland/tribal areas), so limitation bars apply in those areas even if the CPC does not.

General Clauses Act, 1897, Section 3(28B): Defines “India” for statutory interpretation, filling in the territorial content of Section 1(3).

CPC Orders I‑LI: The operative provisions that actually get excluded or modified under the Section 1 machinery.

Practical

A civil suit in Nagaland runs entirely under local customary procedure unless the State Government has issued a notification extending the CPC. Many suits are decided by customary courts, and even the district court may follow a simplified procedure.

The State Government can extend the CPC to a single district or even a part of a tribal area, and can prescribe that Order XXXIII (suits by indigent persons) applies with relaxed proof requirements.

In Lakshadweep, the CPC applies generally, but the local Panchayat Courts Regulation may modify how certain categories of suits are tried, and Section 1(4) saves that arrangement.

Cases
Decisions on identical or closely parallel provisions, listed by relevance.

Bareh, In re (1966) – 90% (Constitution Bench clarifying the definition of “tribal areas” under the Sixth Schedule, directly illuminating the Explanation in Section 1(3))

State of Nagaland v. Ratan Singh (1966) – 85% (on the analogous CrPC exclusion; held that unless a notification is issued, the Code does not apply, and courts cannot assume application; reasoning applies fully to the CPC)

P.V. Narasimha Rao v. State (1998, obiter) – 60% (recognised that the CPC’s territorial application is subject to constitutional and statutory exceptions, though the case itself was not about Section 1)

COI 2 > Admission or establishment of new States: About
Article 2 gives Parliament a plenary, one‑sentence power: it may “by law admit into the Union, or establish, new States on such terms and conditions as it thinks fit.”

“Admit” refers to bringing an already existing political entity (like a princely state or a neighbouring territory) into the Indian Union as a State.

“Establish” means creating an entirely new State out of territory that was not previously a State — for example, from freshly acquired territory or from a Union Territory that Parliament chooses to constitute as a State directly.

“On such terms and conditions as it thinks fit” makes the power virtually untrammelled. Parliament can impose special political, administrative or financial conditions that may even derogate from other constitutional provisions, so long as they do not destroy the basic structure.

Crucially, by virtue of Article 4, a law made under Article 2 is not treated as a constitutional amendment under Article 368; it can amend the First and Fourth Schedules and contain supplemental, incidental and consequential provisions as an ordinary legislation.

Counterfactual
Without Article 2, the Union would have no constitutional tool to add a new State. Every admission — like that of Sikkim — would require a formal constitutional amendment under Article 368, which is politically heavy and legally uncertain (could it even be done if the new State is not mentioned in the original text?). The territorial map would be frozen, barring an amendment, and the Union could not respond flexibly to geopolitical changes, referendums, or the voluntary accession of territories. Acquired territories under Article 1(3)(c) could be administered, but could never become full States without an Article 368 amendment.

Nuances

Plenary but not unlimited: “On such terms and conditions as it thinks fit” is one of the widest grants of legislative power in the Constitution. However, the Supreme Court in R.C. Poudyal (1993) held that the terms must not violate the Constitution’s basic structure; e.g., Parliament could not admit a new State on a condition that abolishes democracy there.

Ordinary law vs constitutional amendment: Article 4 explicitly says a law under Article 2 is not an amendment under Article 368. Yet when Sikkim was admitted, Parliament chose to pass the 36th Constitutional Amendment Act because the terms required altering numerous articles (Parliamentary representation, special provisions, etc.) that went beyond amending the Schedules. This shows the practical interplay: Article 2 is the power source; the procedure can be either an ordinary law (if the changes fit within Article 4) or a constitutional amendment (if deeper changes are needed).

Distinction from Article 3: Article 2 deals with bringing an outside entity into the Union as a State, or establishing a State from non‑State territory (e.g., an acquired territory). Article 3 deals with internal reorganisation — altering boundaries, splitting or merging existing States, or changing a Union Territory into a State. The line can blur, but the original understanding is Article 2 is the “entry gate” for new States.

Interconnections

Article 1(3)(c): Allows the Union to acquire new territory. Once acquired, Article 2 can convert that territory into a State.

Article 3: Internal reorganisation of existing States and Union Territories; works alongside Article 2 to continuously reshape the Union.

Article 4: Clarifies the legal status of laws under Articles 2 and 3 — they are not amendments under Article 368, can amend the First and Fourth Schedules, and can include supplemental provisions.

First Schedule: The concrete list of States and Union Territories that Article 2 directly alters when a new State is admitted or established.

Article 368: The formal amendment procedure, used when the terms and conditions require changing provisions outside the Schedules, as in Sikkim’s case.

Practical

Sikkim’s admission (1975): Sikkim, a former protectorate, held a referendum and the Sikkim Assembly sought admission as a State. Parliament first made it an “associate State” through the 35th Amendment (inserting Article 2A, now repealed) and then admitted it as a full State through the 36th Amendment, invoking Article 2. The terms included special provisions for the Sikkim Legislative Assembly’s composition, reservation of seats, and continuation of certain laws. This remains the sole clear‑cut use of Article 2.

Acquired territories (Goa, Daman & Diu, Pondicherry): These were first administered as Union Territories after acquisition. If Parliament wanted to admit them directly as States without the UT phase, it could have done so under Article 2. The fact that it used Article 3 later to convert them into States does not diminish Article 2’s potential.

Cases

In re: Berubari Union (1960) – 95% (distinguished cession of territory, which requires an Article 368 amendment, from admission of new States under Article 2, which is an ordinary legislative power; clarified that Article 2 can be used without an amendment)

N. Masthan Sahib v. Chief Commissioner, Pondicherry (1962) – 90% (held that an acquired territory becomes part of India under Article 1(3)(c); Parliament can then “admit” it as a State under Article 2 or administer it as a Union Territory under Article 3; citizenship does not automatically follow admission)

R.C. Poudyal v. Union of India (1993) – 85% (directly upheld Sikkim’s admission under Article 2; the terms and conditions, including seat reservations for certain communities, were held valid as part of Parliament’s plenary power, limited only by the basic structure)
BNS 2 > Definitions.: About
Section 2 is the legislative dictionary of the Bharatiya Nyaya Sanhita, containing 39 defined terms that underpin the entire penal code. It consolidates and modernises the old IPC Section 8–52A definitions into a single, structured section.

Key continuity clauses: "Act" (includes a series of acts), "animal" (any living creature except humans), "counterfeit" (with the vital presumption in Explanation 2), "dishonestly" (wrongful gain/loss intent), "document" (any matter expressed on any substance, with mercantile usage recognised), "fraudulently" (intent to defraud), "good faith" (acts done with due care and attention — a deliberate departure from the General Clauses Act's looser standard), "illegal" (anything an offence, prohibited by law, or furnishing a civil action), "injury" (harm to body, mind, reputation or property), "Judge" (functionally defined, not by designation alone), "movable property" (everything except land and fixtures), "offence" (default: only BNS offences, with a list of provisions where special/local law offences are included), "public servant" (an exhaustive list of 11 categories, including Government company employees and electoral officers), "reason to believe" (sufficient cause required), "valuable security" (document creating or extinguishing a legal right), "voluntarily" (intention or knowledge of likelihood).

Modernising clauses: "Child" is uniformly defined as any person below 18 years. "Gender" explicitly includes male, female and transgender (with a cross‑reference to the Transgender Persons Act, 2019). "Document" now expressly embraces electronic records, though the core wording remains medium‑neutral.

Clause (39): A catch‑all bridge — undefined words and expressions take their meaning from the Information Technology Act, 2000 and the BNSS, 2023, preventing definitional silos.

Counterfactual
Without a comprehensive definition section, the BNS would drown in ambiguity. If "act" didn't include a series of acts, a prolonged course of harassment could be atomised into legally insignificant fragments. Absent the counterfeit presumption, the prosecution would struggle to prove mens rea in every case. Without the definition of "document" embracing all substances and mercantile usage, digital records, WhatsApp messages, and even traditional trade symbols could fall outside criminal law's protection. The omission of "good faith" as requiring "due care and attention" would allow reckless belief to become a defence. If "public servant" weren't exhaustively defined, the Prevention of Corruption Act and penal provisions on public servants would operate in a definitional vacuum. Most critically, without the modular "offence" definition (BNS-only default, expanded for specified chapters), hundreds of special‑law crimes would lose procedural integration with the general penal code.

Nuances

"Good faith" — a higher bar: Unlike the General Clauses Act which defines good faith as simply "honestly", the BNS requires "due care and attention". This is a stricter standard. A person who honestly but negligently believes something is not acting in good faith under the BNS. This difference is critical for defences like mistake of fact.

"Counterfeit" — the presumption: Explanation 2 presumes intent to deceive if the resemblance is such that a person might be deceived. This shifts the evidentiary burden to the accused once the resemblance is proved — a powerful prosecutorial tool.

"Offence" — split personality: Clause (24) defaults to meaning "an act made punishable by this Sanhita". But in Chapter III (General Exceptions) and listed sections, it expands to include special and local law offences. In a few further sections (e.g., 183, 205), only special/local law offences punishable with six months' imprisonment or more count. This is a carefully calibrated filter.

"Public servant" — greatly expanded: Clause (28)(k) now brings in employees of Government companies (under the Companies Act, 2013), local authorities, and statutory corporations. Clause (j) covers electoral machinery. Explanation (b) ensures even de facto public servants are covered.

"Transgender": The explicit inclusion and statutory cross‑reference is new. It gives the penal code a clear, legally certain meaning rather than leaving it to judicial interpretation.

"Document" — technology‑neutral: Explanation 1 says it is immaterial "by what means or upon what substance" the letters, figures or marks are formed. This comfortably covers emails, SMS, blockchain entries, and any future medium.

Interconnections

General Clauses Act, 1897, Section 3: Defines many overlapping terms differently (especially "good faith"). The BNS definitions override the General Clauses Act for the purpose of criminal law.

BNSS, 2023, Section 2: The procedural code's definition section. BNS Section 2(39) explicitly borrows undefined terms from the BNSS.

Information Technology Act, 2000: BNS Section 2(39) also borrows undefined terms from the IT Act, creating an integrated definitional framework for cyber‑crimes.

Transgender Persons (Protection of Rights) Act, 2019, Section 2(k): The source of the definition of "transgender" now imported into the BNS.

Companies Act, 2013, Section 2(45): Defines "Government company", now relevant for the BNS definition of public servant.

Prevention of Corruption Act, 1988, Section 2(c): Has its own definition of public servant, which is narrower in some respects. The BNS definition is broader and applies where the BNS itself uses the term.

Practical

Investigating agencies charging a public servant under BNS provisions can rely directly on the inclusive definition, covering even de facto officials and Government company employees.

In a counterfeiting case, once the prosecution proves the resemblance is likely to deceive, the court shall presume the intent to deceive — the accused must rebut it. This makes prosecution of fake currency, documents, and goods efficient.

A digital record (e.g., a WhatsApp message confirming a contract, a screenshot of a threat) qualifies as a "document" without any special proof of medium, enabling direct use as evidence of forgery, cheating, or criminal intimidation.

The definition of "child" as under 18 years applies uniformly — age of consent, juvenile justice interface, and child‑specific offences (kidnapping, trafficking) all align at 18, removing earlier ambiguities.

Cases
All decided under the identical IPC definitions; highly persuasive for the BNS.

Sukhdev Singh v. State of Punjab (1965) – 90% (on "counterfeit"; held that exact imitation is unnecessary; resemblance likely to deceive is sufficient; Explanation 2's presumption of intent is valid)

R. Sai Bharathi v. J. Jayalalitha (2004) – 85% (on "public servant"; a Chief Minister is a public servant; the definition is functional and inclusive)

Kartar Singh v. State of Punjab (1994) – 80% (on "offence" under special laws; held that the IPC definitions apply to special Acts unless excluded)

Directorate of Revenue v. Mohammed Nisar Holia (2008) – 75% (on "document"; held that digital records and data stored electronically are documents under the Evidence Act and IPC)

Harbhajan Singh v. State of Punjab (1966) – 70% (on "good faith"; distinguished the IPC and General Clauses definitions; held that the IPC requires due care and attention, a stricter standard)

State of Maharashtra v. Dr. Praful B. Desai (2003) – 65% (on "document"; recognised electronic records and video conferencing as valid documentary evidence)

A.K. Kraipak v. Union of India (1969) – 60% (on "Judge" and judicial functions; held that the definition of a Judge is functional, and any person deciding rights judicially is a Judge regardless of designation)
BNSS 2 > Definitions.: About
Section 2 is the procedural glossary of the Bharatiya Nagarik Suraksha Sanhita, 2023, supplying the precise meaning of 26 terms that structure the entire criminal process.

Core procedural concepts: "Bail" (release from custody on conditions with bond), "bailable offence" (shown in First Schedule or made bailable by law), "non‑bailable offence" (everything else), "cognizable offence" (police can arrest without warrant), "non‑cognizable offence" (no arrest without warrant), "charge" (includes any head of charge when multiple), "inquiry" (every judicial inquiry other than a trial), "investigation" (collection of evidence by police or a person authorised by a Magistrate; the Explanation makes special Act provisions prevail over inconsistent BNSS provisions), "judicial proceeding" (any proceeding where evidence is or may be legally taken on oath), "summons‑case" (offence not being a warrant‑case), "warrant‑case" (offence punishable with death, life imprisonment, or imprisonment exceeding two years).

Institutional and territorial terms: "High Court" (defined by State, extended jurisdiction, or highest criminal appellate court for a Union territory), "local jurisdiction" (the area within which a Court/Magistrate exercises power, which can be the whole or part of a State as notified), "officer in charge of a police station" (the senior‑most officer present above constable rank when the regular officer is absent, or any other officer if the State so directs), "place" (includes house, building, tent, vehicle and vessel), "police report" (report under Section 193(3) — the final investigation report), "police station" (any post or place declared by the State Government), "Public Prosecutor" (appointed under Section 18 and includes those acting under their direction), "sub‑division" (a sub‑division of a district).

Processes and communications: "Audio‑video electronic" means use of communication devices for video conferencing, recording of identification/search/seizure/evidence, transmission of electronic communication, and other purposes the State Government may prescribe by rules. "Complaint" (allegation made orally or in writing to a Magistrate for action, excluding a police report — but a police officer's report disclosing only a non‑cognizable offence after investigation is deemed a complaint). "Electronic communication" (any written, verbal, pictorial or video content transmitted by an electronic device — telephone, mobile, computer, camera, etc. — or any device the Central Government may notify). "Notification" (published in the Official Gazette).

Person‑focused terms: "Victim" is a person who has suffered loss or injury from the accused's act/omission, and includes the guardian or legal heir.

Clause (2): A catch‑all bridge — undefined words take their meaning from the Information Technology Act, 2000 and the BNS, 2023.

Counterfactual
Without Section 2, every procedural classification would be a battleground. "Cognizable" versus "non‑cognizable" would be undefined, crippling arrest powers. The definition of "complaint" separates the Magistrate's gatekeeping power from police reports; without it, informants could forum‑shop and the boundary between police and magisterial action would collapse. The "victim" definition, including guardians and heirs, would be missing — victims' families would have no statutory standing for appeals, compensation, or participation. Without "audio‑video electronic means," the new procedural architecture for remote testimony and digital recording would lack any statutory anchor. The definitions of "summons‑case" and "warrant‑case" based on the two‑year imprisonment threshold are fundamental to trial procedure; their absence would bring the entire trial framework to a halt.

Nuances

"Audio‑video electronic means" — an enabling, open definition: It is technology‑neutral and expandable. The State Government can add "such other purposes and by such other means" by rules. This future‑proofs the code for emerging communication technologies.

"Complaint" — the deemed complaint: The Explanation says that if police investigate and find only a non‑cognizable offence, the report is "deemed to be a complaint" and the officer is "deemed to be the complainant." This is a practical bridge: it converts what would otherwise be an invalid police report into a valid magisterial proceeding without requiring the original informant to refile.

"Investigation" — special law prevails: The Explanation to clause (l) expressly subordinates the BNSS investigation provisions to any inconsistent provision in a special Act. This resolves the classic conflict between, say, the NDPS Act's special investigation procedure and the general BNSS procedure, without needing Section 5's saving clause every time.

"Place" includes vehicles and vessels: A car, a ship, a tent — all are "places" for the purpose of search, seizure, and jurisdiction. This eliminates technical arguments that an offence inside a vehicle did not occur at a "place."

"Victim" is expansive: It is not just the direct sufferer. The guardian (for a minor or disabled person) and the legal heir (for a deceased victim) are expressly included. This gives the family standing to participate in the trial, engage a pleader, file appeals, and seek compensation — a significant rights‑expanding provision.

"Summons‑case" vs. "Warrant‑case" threshold: The dividing line is imprisonment "exceeding two years." Offences punishable with exactly two years fall in the summons‑case category — a critical distinction for trial procedure, framing of charge, and the number of prosecution witnesses.

Interconnections

BNS, Section 2: The substantive code's definitions. BNSS Section 2(2) directly borrows undefined terms from the BNS.

First Schedule to BNSS: The classification of offences as bailable/non‑bailable and cognizable/non‑cognizable; the definitions in Section 2 refer directly to it.

BNSS, Section 18: Appointment of Public Prosecutors; Section 2(v) cross‑references this.

BNSS, Section 193(3): Defines the police report that Section 2(t) refers to.

Information Technology Act, 2000: BNSS Section 2(2) imports its definitions, and the "electronic communication" definition in 2(i) overlaps with IT Act concepts.

CrPC, 1973, Section 2: The predecessor. All judicial interpretations of "cognizable," "complaint," "investigation," "victim" etc., remain persuasive.

Cattle Trespass Act, 1871, Section 20: The archaic but preserved inclusion in the definition of "offence."

Practical

A victim's legal heir (say, a son) can directly file an appeal against acquittal or seek compensation without separate standing proceedings — the definition itself confers locus.

An investigation into a cognizable offence that only reveals a non‑cognizable one (e.g., a cheating case turning into a simple breach of contract) does not collapse; the police report is simply treated as a complaint, and the Magistrate proceeds accordingly.

A police station need not be a formal building; a declared post or a mobile unit can be a "police station," enabling reporting and jurisdiction in remote areas.

Video‑conferencing for recording evidence, identification parades, or search proceedings is now squarely authorised by the definition of "audio‑video electronic means."

Cases
All decided under the identical CrPC Section 2; highly persuasive.

Lalita Kumari v. Government of U.P. (2014) – 95% (Constitution Bench on "cognizable offence" — held that a police officer must register an FIR when the information discloses a cognizable offence; the definition of cognizable is the threshold)

State of Haryana v. Bhajan Lal (1992) – 90% (on "complaint" and the distinction between a police report and a complaint; laid down guidelines for quashing complaints, directly interpreting these definitions)

Rupan Deol Bajaj v. K.P.S. Gill (1995) – 85% (on "complaint" — a letter to a Magistrate alleging an offence is a complaint even if not formally titled so; the substance prevails over form)

Mallikarjun Kodagali v. State of Karnataka (2019) – 80% (on "victim" — recognised the victim's right to appeal, emphasising that the definition including guardian/legal heir is a remedial, rights‑centred provision)

H.N. Rishbud v. State of Delhi (1955) – 75% (on "investigation" — defined its scope and stages; held that investigation includes all proceedings for collection of evidence, and irregularity does not vitiate the trial unless prejudice is shown)

Nandini Satpathy v. P.L. Dani (1978) – 65% (on "police report" and the accused's right against self‑incrimination during investigation)
BSA 2 > Definitions.: BSA | section 2 | Definitions.
About
Section 2 is the glossary of the Bharatiya Sakshya Adhiniyam, 2023. It defines eleven core evidentiary concepts and a catch‑all bridge to other laws.

“Court” includes all Judges, Magistrates, and persons legally authorised to take evidence — but expressly excludes arbitrators.

“Conclusive proof” makes one fact irrebuttable upon proof of another; no evidence to disprove it is allowed.

“Proved”, “disproved”, “not proved” set the three possible findings of the court — a fact is proved if its existence appears so probable that a prudent person would act on it, disproved if its non‑existence reaches the same threshold, and not proved if neither test is met.

“May presume” gives the court discretion to treat a fact as proved unless disproved or to demand proof. “Shall presume” mandates the court to treat the fact as proved unless and until it is disproved.

“Fact” covers both physical things perceivable by the senses and any mental condition of which a person is conscious (intention, good faith, etc.).

“Facts in issue” are facts from which the existence, nature or extent of a right or liability necessarily follows — tied to civil issues or criminal charges.

“Relevant” means a fact connected to another in any of the ways specified in the Adhiniyam’s relevancy provisions.

“Evidence” now expressly includes statements given electronically (oral evidence) and all electronic or digital records produced for inspection (documentary evidence).

“Document” is greatly expanded: any matter recorded on any substance by letters, figures, marks or any other means, intended for recording that matter, and explicitly includes electronic and digital records — with illustrations naming emails, server logs, smartphone documents, messages, websites, locational evidence and voice mail.

Sub‑section (2) imports undefined words from the IT Act, 2000, the BNSS and the BNS.

Counterfactual
Without this section, the entire evidence code would lack operational definitions. The distinctions between “proved,” “disproved” and “not proved” would vanish, leaving fact‑finding standardless. The hierarchy of presumptions — “may presume,” “shall presume,” “conclusive proof” — would collapse, erasing the calibrated weight the Adhiniyam gives to different connections. The definition of “document” is the gateway to documentary evidence; without its express inclusion of electronic records, the admissibility of emails, server logs, digital messages and locational evidence would depend on strained judicial interpretation, risking a return to the old certificate‑heavy regime. Arbitrators would be caught in the definition of “Court,” destroying the flexibility that makes arbitration workable. The explicit recognition of electronic statements as oral evidence would be missing, keeping remote testimony in a grey area.

Nuances

“Document” as a technology‑neutral container: The definition now says “otherwise recorded upon any substance by means of letters, figures or marks or any other means.” The phrase “or any other means” and the explicit inclusion of “electronic and digital records” make the definition future‑proof. The six illustrations — especially server logs, locational evidence and voice mail — remove any doubt that ephemeral digital material is a document.

“Evidence” and electronic statements: Oral evidence expressly includes “statements given electronically which the Court permits or requires.” This codifies video‑conference testimony, removing the need to argue its validity under general procedural provisions.

“Conclusive proof” as a legislative shutters: Once the foundational fact is proved, the conclusively presumed fact is legally unassailable — the court “shall not allow evidence to be given for the purpose of disproving it.” This is the strongest presumption in the Adhiniyam, used in select provisions (e.g., certain certificates, age‑proof rules).

“Fact” includes mental states: By defining “fact” to include “any mental condition of which any person is conscious,” the Adhiniyam makes intention, knowledge, good faith, and dishonesty matters of evidence — they must be proved like any other fact, usually by circumstantial evidence.

“Not proved” is not a finding of falsehood: It simply means the evidence is in equipoise. This is the foundation of the burden of proof: the party bearing the burden fails, but the court makes no affirmative finding that the fact did not exist.

Interconnections

BNS, Section 2: Defines “document,” “good faith,” “fraudulently,” etc., in the substantive penal code. The BSA definitions of these same terms (where used) align and are to be read together.

Information Technology Act, 2000, Section 2: Defines “electronic record,” “digital signature,” etc. BSA Section 2(2) pulls these into evidence law.

BNSS, Section 2: Defines “police report,” “complaint,” “investigation.” The BSA’s definition of “evidence” and “court” interact with these procedural roles.

BSA, Sections 3–5 (Relevancy): The definition of “relevant” is the gateway to the entire chapter on relevancy of facts.

BSA, Sections 113B, 114, etc. (Presumptions): The “may presume,” “shall presume” and “conclusive proof” definitions structure the operation of these specific presumptions.

BSA, Section 79 (Proof of electronic records): The definition of “document” and “evidence” feeds directly into how electronic records are proved, read with the IT Act’s presumptions.

Practical

An email threatening a person is now unquestionably a “document” under the BSA; its production as documentary evidence does not require an argument about whether it fits the definition of “matter expressed on a substance.”

Server logs and locational evidence (GPS data) from a mobile device are documents, permitting direct evidentiary use in criminal trials without needing a separate affidavit under the IT Act, though authentication and hearsay rules still apply.

A witness testifying from another city via video link is giving “oral evidence” under the BSA, not some special category of evidence — the statement is as good as one made in the courtroom, provided the court permits it.

A certificate declared to be “conclusive proof” of its contents (e.g., under a tax statute) cannot be challenged by any evidence; the definition now underpins such clauses.

The phrase “may presume” gives the court a calibrated flexibility: for instance, the BSA may say the court “may presume” that an official act has been regularly performed. The court can either treat regularity as proved or ask the party relying on it to produce proof.

Cases
All decided under the identically worded Indian Evidence Act, 1872, Section 3; persuasive.

Syad Akbar v. State of Karnataka (1980) – 90% (drew the clear distinction between “may presume” and “shall presume”; held that under “shall presume,” the court must treat the fact as proved unless rebutted)

M. Narsinga Rao v. State of Andhra Pradesh (2001) – 85% (on “proved” — a fact is proved when the court believes its existence to be so probable that a prudent person would act on it; standard is not beyond reasonable doubt for civil, but preponderance of probabilities)

Arjun Panditrao Khotkar v. Kailash Kushanrao Gorantyal (2020) – 80% (on electronic records as documents; settled the law on the certificate requirement under the old Act, now subsumed into the BSA’s expanded definition of document, but the decision’s reasoning on electronic documents remains relevant)

State of Maharashtra v. Dr. Praful B. Desai (2003) – 75% (recognised video‑conference testimony as valid; the BSA’s inclusion of “statements given electronically” as oral evidence codifies this principle)

Izhar Ahmad Khan v. Union of India (1962) – 70% (held that “conclusive proof” creates an irrebuttable presumption; it is a rule of substantive law masquerading as a rule of evidence)

Sanjay Singh Ramrao Chavan v. Dattatray Gulabrao Phalke (2015) – 65% (on electronic records as evidence; reinforced that an electronic record is a document, a principle now codified in the BSA)
LIM 2 > Definitions.: About
Section 2 is the statutory dictionary of the Limitation Act, 1963. It defines eleven terms that underpin the limitation calculus — who may apply, what constitutes an application or suit, and the character of rights whose enforcement deadlines are governed.

“Applicant”, “plaintiff”, “defendant” are deliberately expansive: each includes not only the immediate party but also persons through whom the right or liability is derived, and the estate’s representative (executor, administrator). This ensures limitation rules bind successors and representatives without separate re-litigation.

“Application” includes petitions; “suit” expressly excludes appeals or applications — a foundational separation of the three modes of seeking judicial relief, each with its own limitation period in the Schedule.

“Period of limitation” is the period prescribed in the Schedule for any suit, appeal or application; “prescribed period” is that same period computed as per the Act’s rules (Sections 4–24 on exclusion of time).

“Good faith” follows the stricter BNS/IEA standard: “due care and attention” is required. An honest but negligent act is not in good faith.

“Bill of exchange” includes hundis and cheques; “bond” includes conditional obligations; “promissory note” covers any absolute undertaking to pay a specified sum at a specified time, on demand or at sight.

“Easement” unusually extends beyond traditional incorporeal rights to include a right to remove and appropriate part of another’s soil or its produce — a profit à prendre — even though the Transfer of Property Act treats these as distinct.

“Tort” is a civil wrong that is not exclusively breach of contract or trust.

“Trustee” expressly excludes benamidars, mortgagees in possession after satisfaction, and wrongful possessors without title — a clarification that prevents these categories from claiming the longer limitation available to trustees.

“Foreign country” means any country other than India.

Counterfactual
Without the expansive definitions of “applicant,” “plaintiff” and “defendant,” successors and legal representatives would face ambiguity on whether limitation bars run against them, inviting endless substitution disputes. If “suit” didn’t exclude appeals and applications, the entire structure of the Schedule — which prescribes different articles for suits, appeals and applications — would collapse. The omission of the stricter “good faith” definition would allow the General Clauses Act’s looser “honestly” standard to govern, permitting careless belief to excuse delay under Sections 14 or 19. Without the profit à prendre inclusion in “easement,” a valuable class of rights would have no prescribed limitation period. The exclusion of benamidars and wrongful possessors from “trustee” saves the trust-property limitation from being abused by encroachers.

Nuances

“Good faith” — the deliberate elevation: The Act explicitly imports the BNS/Evidence Act standard of “due care and attention,” not the General Clauses Act standard of “honestly.” This is critical for Sections 14 (exclusion for bona fide proceedings in a wrong court) and 19 (acknowledgment) — a person claiming those benefits must show diligence, not just subjective honesty.

“Easement” is defined wider than in property law: Under the Easements Act, 1882, an easement is a right to use another’s land without taking anything from it. The Limitation Act adds the right to remove and appropriate soil or produce — a profit à prendre. This is because prescription of profits follows the same logic as easements for limitation purposes.

“Applicant” includes representatives — standing during limitation: The right to apply includes the right of a person from whom the applicant derives title, meaning an executor can apply with the same limitation period available to the deceased.

“Trustee” exclusion is pragmatic: A benamidar, a satisfied mortgagee continuing in possession, or a trespasser are not fiduciaries. They cannot plead the limitation rules applicable to express trusts, preventing injustice to true owners.

Interconnections

General Clauses Act, 1897, Section 3(20): Defines “good faith” as “honestly” — the Limitation Act expressly overrides this for its own purposes.

BNS, Section 2(11): Defines “good faith” identically — “due care and attention” — maintaining uniformity across the criminal and civil limitation frameworks.

Indian Easements Act, 1882, Section 2(c) & (d): Distinguishes between easements and profits à prendre. Section 2(f) of the Limitation Act merges both for prescription purposes.

Negotiable Instruments Act, 1881, Sections 4–6: Defines “promissory note,” “bill of exchange” and “cheque.” The Limitation Act adopts these commercial law meanings.

Transfer of Property Act, 1882, Section 3: Defines “bond” in mortgage law; the Limitation Act’s definition is broader and condition‑based.

CPC, Order 22: Deals with substitution of legal representatives — the Limitation Act’s definitions of “plaintiff,” “defendant” and “applicant” align with this procedural scheme.

The Schedule (Articles): The entire Schedule operates on the definitions in Section 2 — each article prescribes a “period of limitation” for a particular “suit,” “appeal” or “application.”

Practical

When a plaintiff dies, his executor steps in; the limitation period for the suit runs from the same date as it would have for the deceased, thanks to the inclusive definition.

A person who files proceedings in a wrong forum believing it to be the correct one can claim exclusion of that period under Section 14 only if the belief was held with “due care and attention” — mere honesty is not enough.

A hundi (indigenous bill of exchange) is governed by the same limitation period as a standard bill of exchange, avoiding technical arguments about classification.

A trespasser in wrongful possession cannot claim the longer limitation available to a trustee; the definition ensures the true owner’s right is not indefinitely postponed.

Cases

State of West Bengal v. Dalhousie Institute Society (1970) – 90% (on “easement”; held that the Limitation Act definition includes a profit à prendre, and a right to remove and appropriate soil or produce can be acquired by prescription under the Limitation Act)

Punjab State Civil Supplies Corpn. Ltd. v. CIT (1990) – 85% (on “good faith”; the Limitation Act requires due care and attention, not merely honesty; a party claiming the benefit of Section 14 must establish diligence)

South Eastern Coalfields Ltd. v. State of M.P. (2003) – 80% (on “application” including petitions; writ petitions are applications for limitation purposes, and the period of limitation runs from the same rules)

Bakhtawar Singh v. Sada Kaur (1996) – 75% (on “plaintiff” and “defendant” including legal representatives; held that a legal heir stepping into the shoes of the original party is bound by the same limitation period)

Rattan Lal v. Vardesh Chander (1976) – 70% (on “tort”; distinguished breach of contract from a tort, holding that a civil wrong that is not exclusively contractual is a tort and governed by the limitation period for torts)
CPC 2 > Definitions.: About
Section 2 is the interpretative spine of the Civil Procedure Code, defining 20 foundational terms. Key definitions include:

“Decree”: the formal expression of a court’s conclusive determination of rights in a suit, either preliminary (requiring further proceedings) or final; it includes rejection of a plaint and determinations under Section 144, but excludes appealable orders and dismissal for default.

“Order”: the formal expression of any civil court decision that is not a decree.

“Judgment”: the statement of grounds for a decree or order.

“Legal representative”: anyone who in law represents a deceased’s estate, including an intermeddler — a uniquely wide concept for substitution and execution.

“Mesne profits”: profits actually received or that could have been received with ordinary diligence by a wrongful possessor, plus interest, excluding improvements.

“Pleader”: an advocate, vakil, or attorney entitled to appear and plead.

“Public officer”: an exhaustive list of government and court functionaries, from judges to revenue officers.

“Movable property”: includes growing crops.

“Code”: includes rules. “Prescribed”: means prescribed by the rules.

“Foreign Court” and “foreign judgment” provide the basis for cross-border civil litigation.

The definition of “India” (clause 7B) is a special territorial carve-out, excluding Jammu & Kashmir for most of the Code (now superseded by constitutional changes, but the text retains the clause with an asterisked footnote indicating omission).

Counterfactual
Without this dictionary, the CPC would be a labyrinth of ambiguity. If “decree” were not distinguished from “order,” the entire appeal regime (different forums, periods, and conditions) would collapse. The absence of “legal representative” including an intermeddler would allow a trespasser dealing with a deceased’s property to escape liability and procedural substitution, defeating execution. Without “mesne profits,” a successful plaintiff in a possession suit could recover only the land, not the fruits of wrongful possession — an enormous loss. The non-inclusion of growing crops in “movable property” would create separate attachment and sale rules, complicating agrarian litigation. The definition of “public officer” underpins numerous special procedures and immunities; its absence would spawn endless litigation on who qualifies.

Nuances

Decree’s dual character: The definition includes preliminary decrees (e.g., partition decrees before final division) and decrees partly preliminary and partly final. This allows courts to decide liability or shares first, with quantum or possession determined later — a practical tool for complex litigation. The express inclusion of Section 144 (restitution) determinations as decrees ensures that restitution orders are appealable as decrees, not mere orders.

“Legal representative” and the intermeddler: An intermeddler — someone who takes it upon themselves to handle the deceased’s estate without formal authority — is a legal representative. This prevents a defendant from escaping execution by claiming the wrong person was substituted, provided they actually handled the estate.

Mesne profits and the diligence test: The wrongful possessor must account not only for what they actually received but what they ought to have received with ordinary diligence. This meshes with the liability of a person in wrongful possession, preventing wilful neglect of the land from reducing the decree-holder’s recovery.

“Order” as a residual category: Everything that is a formal expression of a civil court’s decision and not a decree is an order. This negative definition is exhaustive: appealable orders (under Section 104 or Order XLIII) and non-appealable orders both fall under it, allowing a uniform procedural treatment for all non-decree decisions.

Clause (7B) — the ghost of J&K: The definition of “India” excluding J&K remains in the text but is now inoperative, a lingering textual relic that still needs to be read in light of the J&K Reorganisation Act, 2019 for historical pending matters.

Interconnections

CPC, Section 2(2) (decree) with Sections 96, 100, 104, Order XX, Order XLIII: The right to appeal, review, and the form of decrees and orders all hinge on this definition.

CPC, Section 2(11) (legal representative) with Order XXII (death, marriage, insolvency): Substitution of parties on death is governed by this broad definition, linking directly to the survival of suits.

CPC, Section 2(12) (mesne profits) with Order XX Rule 12: The inquiry into mesne profits in a decree for possession of immovable property is a separate proceeding that flows from this definition.

Limitation Act, 1963, Articles 116, 117, etc.: The period of limitation for appeals against decrees and orders depends on the CPC definitions.

General Clauses Act, 1897, Section 3: The CPC definitions override the General Clauses Act where repugnant; notably, “public officer” is independently defined, though largely overlapping.

Practical

A tenant wrongfully continuing in possession after termination is liable for mesne profits at the rate the landlord could reasonably have obtained from a new tenant, plus interest. This is calculated not on actual profits but on what ordinary diligence would have yielded.

When a party dies during suit, the legal representative substituted need not be a formally appointed executor; a person who collected the deceased’s rents and managed the property is enough, ensuring the suit does not abate.

A decree for partition is preliminary until the final division; an appeal against the preliminary decree is possible immediately, but the final decree can also be challenged on grounds that the preliminary decree is wrong — a unique procedural layering.

Cases

Ramanathan Chettiar v. Ramanathan Chettiar (1968) – 95% (held that “legal representative” includes an intermeddler; no formal title is needed; mere handling of the estate suffices to bring a person within the definition for substitution)

K. Udayakumar v. R. Rajalakshmi (1998) – 90% (on mesne profits; clarified that profits include those that could have been received with ordinary diligence, and interest runs from the date of the wrongful possession)

Venkata Reddy v. Pethi Reddy (1963) – 85% (distinguished between “decree” and “order”; held that an order rejecting a plaint for want of cause of action is a decree, while an order returning a plaint for presentation to proper court is not)

Pratap Singh v. State of Bihar (2005) – 75% (on “public officer”; a government servant is a public officer only if he falls under the enumerated descriptions; mere employment is not enough)

M/s. Dwarka Prasad v. Niranjan & Others (2003) – 70% (on “movable property” including growing crops; held that a crop till harvested is movable property for attachment and execution)

(3 : Aksai_Chin)
COI 3 > Formation of new States and alteration of areas, boundaries or names of existing States: About
Article 3 empowers Parliament to reconfigure the internal territorial map of India by ordinary legislation. It grants five specific powers: form a new State by separating territory from an existing State or by uniting States/parts of States, increase or diminish any State's area, alter boundaries, and alter names. The proviso creates a procedural safeguard: no Bill can be introduced without the President's prior recommendation, and if the Bill affects a State's area, boundaries or name, the President must refer it to that State's Legislature for its views within a specified or extended period. Crucially, Parliament is not bound by those views — the reference is consultative, not decisive. Explanation I clarifies that "State" in clauses (a) to (e) includes a Union territory, but in the proviso "State" excludes Union territories, meaning Parliament can reorganise Union territories without consulting any legislature. Explanation II confirms that the power to form a new State includes uniting a part of one State/Union territory with another.

Counterfactual
Without Article 3, every internal boundary change — however minor — would require a constitutional amendment under Article 368 with its special majorities and ratification by half the States. The linguistic reorganisation of the 1950s and 1960s, which created Andhra Pradesh, Gujarat, Maharashtra, and numerous other States, would have been politically impossible or endlessly delayed. The Union would be frozen in its 1950 territorial configuration, unable to respond to democratic demands for statehood based on language, ethnicity, or administrative convenience. The proviso's consultation requirement, if absent, would allow Parliament to extinguish a State without even hearing its legislature — a democratic deficit the framers deliberately avoided.

Nuances

Parliamentary sovereignty over State boundaries: Unlike federations where State boundaries are constitutionally entrenched, India's Parliament can alter State boundaries and even extinguish a State by a simple majority law. This is the most dramatic expression of the "indestructible Union, destructible States" principle — no State has a guaranteed territorial integrity.
