Consultative, not binding: The proviso makes the State Legislature's views a procedural prerequisite, not a substantive veto. Parliament can, and has, legislated in the teeth of State Legislature opposition. The Supreme Court in Babulal Parate (1961) held that Parliament is not bound by the views, and the reference period need not await the legislature's actual resolution — the expiry of the specified time is enough.

Explanation I's dual meaning of "State": For the substantive power (clauses a–e), "State" includes Union territories — Parliament can merge, divide or rename a Union territory just as it can a State. But for the procedural safeguard (proviso), Union territories are excluded; no local legislature need be consulted, reflecting their constitutionally different status.

Explanation II — a clarifying, not enlarging, power: It confirms that uniting a part of one State/UT with another State/UT is "formation of a new State," resolving any doubt that clause (a) covers partial unions as well as complete mergers. This was used extensively in creating linguistic States.

Law under Article 3 is not a constitutional amendment (Article 4): A law made under Article 3 can amend the First and Fourth Schedules and include supplemental provisions without following the Article 368 procedure. This makes territorial reorganisation administratively swift.

Interconnections

Article 2: Deals with admission or establishment of new States from outside the Union. Article 3 covers internal reorganisation. Together they are the twin engines of India's territorial flexibility.

Article 4: Provides that laws under Articles 2 and 3 are not constitutional amendments and can amend the First and Fourth Schedules — the procedural key that makes Article 3 practically workable.

First Schedule: The list of States and Union Territories; every Article 3 law amends this Schedule.

Article 368: The formal amendment procedure deliberately bypassed by Article 3 read with Article 4, except where the reorganisation requires changes to provisions beyond the Schedules.

State Reorganisation Act, 1956: The foundational statute exercising Article 3 powers wholesale, creating linguistic States and redrawing India's internal map.

Article 5: Citizenship provisions are affected by State reorganisation, as domicile and territory change; Parliament typically provides for citizenship adjustments in reorganisation laws.

Practical

The creation of Telangana (2014) involved the Andhra Pradesh Reorganisation Act, passed despite the Andhra Pradesh Legislature's rejection of the reference. The President's reference was made, the period expired, and Parliament legislated — a textbook Article 3 operation.

The alteration of a State's name — e.g., Orissa to Odisha, Bombay to Mumbai (though the State name was already Maharashtra, the city's official name change had cascading legal effects) — requires only a Bill with Presidential recommendation and reference to the State Legislature. No boundary change, no land transfer, just a legislative name change.

The merger of Dadra and Nagar Haveli with Daman and Diu (2019) as a single Union territory was done under Article 3's Union territory-inclusive power; no local legislature was consulted.

Cases

Babulal Parate v. State of Bombay (1959) – 95% (Constitution Bench; held that the proviso's reference to the State Legislature is not mandatory in the sense that Parliament must wait for the legislature's actual resolution; Parliament can proceed once the specified or extended period expires; Parliament is not bound by the views expressed)

S.R. Bommai v. Union of India (1994) – 80% (while primarily on Article 356, it extensively discussed the "Union of States" and federal balance; held that the power under Article 3 does not permit destroying the federal structure or basic features; a State cannot be wiped out altogether — though a full extinction would likely trigger basic structure review, and Bommai did not directly rule on Article 3)

Mullaperiyar Environmental Protection Forum v. Union of India (2006) – 65% (recognised Parliament's plenary power under Article 3 to alter boundaries, including for purposes like implementing inter‑State river water agreements; the power is not limited to linguistic or cultural reorganisation)
BNS 3 > General explanations.: About
Section 3 is the interpretive master‑key to the entire penal code. It lays down nine general principles that govern how every other provision is to be read.

Sub‑section (1) — General Exceptions apply universally: Every definition of an offence, every penal provision, and every illustration is automatically subject to the General Exceptions (Chapter III), even where the exception is not repeated. The two illustrations make this concrete: a child under seven cannot commit an offence, though the individual offence sections never mention this; a police officer lawfully arresting a murderer is not guilty of wrongful confinement.

Sub‑section (2) — Uniformity of expressions: Any expression explained anywhere in the Sanhita carries that meaning throughout the entire code.

Sub‑section (3) — Vicarious possession: Property in the possession of a spouse, clerk or servant on behalf of another person is in that other person's possession. A temporary or occasional clerk/servant counts.

Sub‑section (4) — Acts include omissions: Words referring to "acts done" extend to illegal omissions unless the context shows a contrary intention.

Sub‑section (5) — Common intention / joint liability: When several persons act in furtherance of a common intention, each is liable for the act as if they did it alone. This is the statutory foundation of constructive criminal liability — no need to prove who struck the fatal blow.

Sub‑section (6) — Joint acts with criminal knowledge/intention: Where an act is criminal only because of a specific knowledge or intention, each person who joins in with that same knowledge or intention is liable as if they acted alone.

Sub‑section (7) — Mixed act and omission: If causing an effect (or attempting it) by an act alone or by omission alone is an offence, causing it partly by act and partly by omission is the same offence. The illustration clarifies: intentionally killing by both beating (act) and starving (omission) is still one murder.

Sub‑section (8) — Co‑operation by separate acts: When an offence is committed through several acts, anyone who intentionally co‑operates by doing any one of those acts — singly or jointly — commits the entire offence. The three illustrations progressively clarify: (a) two people administering separate poison doses are both guilty of murder; (b) two jailors alternating shifts and each starving the prisoner are both guilty of murder; (c) a jailor who starves but does not co‑operate with the successor jailor (who finishes the killing) is guilty only of attempt, while the successor is guilty of murder.

Sub‑section (9) — Different offences from the same act: Several persons engaged in a criminal act may be guilty of different offences. The illustration is classic: A kills under grave provocation (culpable homicide); B joins in with murderous intent. B is guilty of murder, A only of culpable homicide — same killing, different degrees of guilt.

Counterfactual
Without Section 3, every penal section would have to list every applicable General Exception or risk catching innocent conduct. A police officer arresting a suspect would have to point to a specific exception written into the wrongful confinement section — a drafting impossibility. Without sub‑section (5), the prosecution would have to prove individual causation for every participant in a group crime, gutting joint‑liability prosecutions. The omission of sub‑section (7) would mean that killing by a combination of acts and omissions might escape punishment because neither the "act" nor "omission" offence perfectly captured the mixed conduct. Sub‑section (8) prevents the absurdity of each co‑operator in a multi‑act offence being liable only for their fragment, not the completed crime. Sub‑section (9) prevents the crude result that every participant in a killing must be convicted of the same offence regardless of individual circumstances. The code would be a collection of discrete definitions without a unifying architecture.

Nuances

Sub‑section (1) — The invisible shield: General Exceptions operate silently. Even if a section says "whoever causes death… shall be punished," the reader must mentally insert "…unless the act is done in self‑defence, by a child under seven, by a person of unsound mind, etc." The burden is on the defence to raise the exception, but once raised, the prosecution must disprove it beyond reasonable doubt. This is a unique structural choice — exceptions are not built into definitions but overlay the entire code.

Sub‑section (5) — Common intention as a threshold: The phrase "in furtherance of the common intention" requires a pre‑arranged plan or meeting of minds, not mere simultaneous action. The common intention can be formed on the spot, seconds before the act. It is distinct from "common object" (Section 190) which requires membership of an unlawful assembly. Each participant's liability is full and primary, not secondary or accessorial.

Sub‑section (6) — A narrower joint‑liability rule: Unlike sub‑section (5), sub‑section (6) applies only where the criminality of the act depends on a specific mental state (knowledge or intention). Each co‑participant must individually possess that mental state. If only one participant has the requisite knowledge and the others are innocent tools, the latter are not liable.

Sub‑section (8) — The co‑operation must be intentional: Each co‑operator must intend to co‑operate in the commission of the offence. Illustration (c) makes this precise: A and B did not co‑operate (no collusion, no communication); therefore A is liable only for attempt, B for the completed murder. This distinguishes pre‑arranged multi‑act crimes from sequential independent acts.

Sub‑section (9) — Individualised guilt: This is a rule of justice, not a rule of liability. It prevents the prosecution from mechanically charging all participants with the same offence, forcing courts to examine each person's mental state and culpability separately.

Interconnections

BNS, Chapter III (General Exceptions, Sections 14–44): The substantive exceptions that Section 3(1) makes universally applicable — mistake of fact, judicial acts, accident, necessity, infancy, insanity, intoxication, consent, good faith, private defence, etc.

BNS, Section 190 (unlawful assembly / common object): A distinct basis for constructive liability, requiring membership in an unlawful assembly and an act in furtherance of its common object. Section 3(5) applies even without an unlawful assembly.

BNS, Section 2 (definitions of "act," "illegal," "omission," "voluntarily"): These definitions supply the building blocks that Section 3's interpretive rules operate upon.

BNSS, Section 223 (joint trial): The procedural framework for trying persons jointly charged under Section 3(5) common intention.

BSA, Section 8 (evidence of common intention): The evidence law provision governing how common intention is proved — typically through circumstantial evidence, conduct, and surrounding circumstances.

Practical

In a gang‑rape prosecution, the prosecution need not prove which accused committed which specific act. Under sub‑section (5), all participants acting in furtherance of the common intention are equally liable for the completed gang‑rape. This eliminates the "who did what" defence in group sexual violence.

A husband who controls the family property through his wife, who holds it as his nominee, is in "possession" of that property under sub‑section (3) for purposes of theft, criminal misappropriation, or dishonest delivery — even if he never physically touches it.

A caretaker who deliberately fails to feed a bedridden person, combined with a single act of violence, commits murder under sub‑section (7) — the act and omission combine to form a single offence.

The "General Exceptions" overlay means that even without a specific self‑defence clause in the murder section, an accused can argue self‑defence, and the court must consider it.

Cases
All decided under the identical IPC sections (mostly Section 34 IPC for common intention, Section 6 for General Exceptions, etc.); highly persuasive.

Mahbub Shah v. Emperor (1945, PC) – 95% (Privy Council, the locus classicus on common intention; held that common intention requires a pre‑arranged plan; mere simultaneous action or similar intention is not enough; each participant must share the intention and act in furtherance of it)

K.M. Nanavati v. State of Maharashtra (1962) – 85% (examined General Exceptions, specifically the grave and sudden provocation defence; reinforced that Section 3(1) makes all exceptions applicable to every offence, and the burden of proof lies on the prosecution once the defence raises a plausible case)

Barendra Kumar Ghosh v. Emperor (1925, PC) – 80% (held that under the common intention rule, each participant is fully liable for the principal act; the distinction between principal and accessory is irrelevant; a person who stands guard while others commit murder is equally guilty of murder)

Jai Bhagwan v. State of Haryana (1999) – 75% (distinguished common intention from common object; held that common intention may be formed moments before the act, but must be proved; mere presence at the scene is insufficient)

Harish Chandra v. State of Rajasthan (1975) – 70% (applied sub‑section (9) — same act, different offences; held that individual participants in a criminal act may be convicted of different degrees of homicide based on their individual mens rea)
BNSS 3 > Construction of references.: About
Section 3 is a statutory rule of interpretation that translates all references to "Magistrate" in any law (not just the BNSS) into the BNSS's binary classification of Judicial and Executive Magistrates.

Sub-section (1) provides the default rule: any reference in any law to a "Magistrate" without qualifying words, or to a "Magistrate of the first class" or "Magistrate of the second class," shall be read as referring to a Judicial Magistrate of the corresponding class exercising jurisdiction in that area. This creates a presumption that references to Magistrates are references to Judicial Magistrates, unless the context indicates otherwise.

Sub-section (2) then separates functions by their nature. Clause (a) assigns "judicial functions" — those involving appreciation or sifting of evidence, decisions exposing a person to punishment, penalty or detention, or sending a person for trial — exclusively to Judicial Magistrates. Clause (b) assigns "administrative or executive functions" — granting, suspending or cancelling licences, sanctioning or withdrawing prosecutions — to Executive Magistrates. This functional bifurcation is subject to the BNSS's own provisions and, in the case of clause (b), subject to clause (a), meaning that if an ostensibly administrative function has a judicial element, it defaults to the Judicial Magistrate.

Counterfactual
Without Section 3, the separation between Judicial and Executive Magistrates — a foundational reform of the 1973 CrPC — would be undone every time a pre‑1973 law or a special law referred to a "Magistrate" simpliciter. Executive Magistrates could be called upon to try offences, evaluate evidence and order detention, merging executive and judicial power in violation of the separation principle. Conversely, Judicial Magistrates could be burdened with purely administrative tasks like issuing arms licences or sanctioning prosecutions, for which they have no training or infrastructure. The entire scheme of functionally distinct magistracies would collapse into confusion, with different courts reaching different conclusions on whether an old statute's "Magistrate" means a Judicial or Executive Magistrate.

Nuances

"Unless the context otherwise requires" is the escape valve: if a special law's context clearly contemplates an Executive Magistrate (e.g., certain provisions of the Revenue Code or land acquisition laws), the default judicial reading yields. But the burden is on the party arguing for the Executive Magistrate to show the contrary context.

Functional, not designation‑based, allocation: Sub-section (2) looks at what the Magistrate actually does — sifting evidence, imposing punishment, ordering detention — not the label of the power. A single statute may require both types of Magistrate for different sections; Section 3 provides the sorting algorithm.

The "subject to clause (a)" subordination in clause (b): Even within a predominantly administrative function, if a specific component involves appreciation of evidence or exposes a person to a penalty, that component must go to a Judicial Magistrate. This is a safety valve preventing the executive branch from encroaching on judicial terrain through broadly worded administrative powers.

Detention "pending investigation, inquiry or trial" is judicial: Clause (a) explicitly categorises the power to detain a person during investigation, inquiry or trial (i.e., remand power) as a judicial function. This codifies the separation principle that the power to deprive a person of liberty during criminal proceedings belongs exclusively to Judicial Magistrates.

Sanction and withdrawal of prosecution are executive: Clause (b) expressly places sanctioning prosecution and withdrawing from prosecution in the executive domain, consistent with the understanding that these are policy‑driven decisions by the executive branch, not adjudicatory acts.

Interconnections

CrPC, 1973, Section 3: The identical predecessor provision. The entire body of case law on the Judicial/Executive Magistrate separation applies.

BNSS, Sections 6–23: Establish the hierarchy and jurisdiction of Judicial Magistrates and Executive Magistrates — the institutional framework into which Section 3 translates references.

BNSS, Section 2 (definitions of "Judicial Magistrate," "Executive Magistrate"): Though the BNSS Section 2 does not separately define these terms, the classification in Sections 6–23 gives them content. Section 3 ensures that external laws align with this classification.

Constitution, Article 50: The Directive Principle requiring separation of the judiciary from the executive in the public services of the State — Section 3 is the statutory implementation of this constitutional mandate.

Pre‑1973 special laws (e.g., Essential Commodities Act, 1955; Customs Act, 1962): These laws use the old "Magistrate" terminology. Section 3 ensures they are read harmoniously with the post‑1973 BNSS/CrPC framework without needing individual amendments.

Practical

A special law like the Railway Property (Unlawful Possession) Act, 1966, uses the term "Magistrate" without qualification. Under Section 3(1), this is read as "Judicial Magistrate of the first class," ensuring trials under that Act are conducted by Judicial Magistrates, not Executive Magistrates.

The power to record a confession under Section 183 BNSS is a judicial function; even if an old law refers to a "Magistrate" recording a statement, Section 3(2)(a) ensures it is a Judicial Magistrate who records it.

The power to issue a search warrant under a regulatory statute is administrative and would fall to an Executive Magistrate under Section 3(2)(b) unless the search involves appreciation of evidence at the pre‑warrant stage that crosses into judicial territory.

When a District Collector is designated as a "Magistrate" under a local law for licensing purposes, Section 3(2)(b) confirms this as an Executive Magistrate function, not requiring a Judicial Magistrate.

Cases
All decided under the identical CrPC Section 3; highly persuasive.

State of Uttar Pradesh v. Kaushaliya (1964) – 85% (though pre‑1973, the reasoning on the need for judicial oversight of punitive measures influenced the drafting of Section 3 and the separation of judicial and executive functions)

Krishna Lal v. State of Haryana (1985) – 80% (held that under the CrPC Section 3, any Magistrate exercising jurisdiction under a special law that involves trial and punishment must be a Judicial Magistrate; an Executive Magistrate cannot try an offence)

State of Gujarat v. Chaturbhuj Maganlal (1976) – 75% (interpreted the transitional provisions after the 1973 CrPC separation; held that references to "Magistrate" in pre‑1973 statutes must be read in light of Section 3, allocating functions appropriately)

S.D.M. v. State of Maharashtra (2005) – 70% (held that the power to order detention during investigation is judicial; an Executive Magistrate cannot exercise remand powers; the "context otherwise requires" exception must be narrowly construed)
BSA 3 > Evidence may be given of facts in issue and relevant facts.: About
Section 3 is the foundational gateway to the entire law of evidence. It states a single, deceptively simple rule: in any suit or proceeding, evidence may be given only of (a) the existence or non‑existence of every fact in issue, and (b) such other facts as are declared relevant by the Adhiniyam — and of no others. This is the exclusionary principle that separates admissible from inadmissible evidence. The Explanation then subordinates this rule to civil procedure: if a party is disentitled to prove a fact by any provision of the CPC (for instance, a document not disclosed at the right stage), Section 3 does not override that procedural bar. Illustration (a) shows facts in issue in a murder trial: the beating, the causing of death, and the intention. Illustration (b) shows the procedural bar: a party who fails to produce a bond at the first hearing cannot rely on Section 3 to produce it later, because the CPC's procedural rules prevail.

Counterfactual
Without Section 3, there would be no boundary between admissible and inadmissible evidence. Courts could receive any information — rumour, hearsay, irrelevant prejudice, bad character, unrelated transactions — and the trial would become an unstructured fishing expedition. The cardinal rule that only facts in issue and facts relevant under the Adhiniyam may be proved is the organising principle of the entire evidence code; without it, Sections 4–55 (relevancy) would be a catalogue without a command. The Explanation's subordination to civil procedure prevents the evidence law from becoming a tool to ambush opponents with undisclosed documents; without it, procedural default rules in the CPC could be circumvented by invoking the broad language of Section 3.

Nuances

"And of no others" — the exclusionary command: These four words are the most powerful in the section. They convert the Adhiniyam from a permissive code (here is what you may prove) into an exclusive code (you may prove only this). Anything outside facts in issue and declared relevant facts is inadmissible, however logically compelling it may seem.

Facts in issue vs. relevant facts: "Facts in issue" are determined by the pleadings (civil) or the charge (criminal) — they are the disputed facts that must be proved to establish the claim or defence. "Relevant facts" are those declared by the Adhiniyam (Sections 4–55) to be connected to the facts in issue — they are the circumstantial, explanatory, or corroborative facts that help the court decide the facts in issue. Section 3 permits evidence of both categories, but only these two categories.

The Explanation — civil procedure as a gatekeeper: The Explanation is a deference clause. It says that even if a fact is logically a fact in issue or relevant, if the CPC (or any procedural law) disentitles the party from proving it — e.g., a document not filed with the plaint under Order VII Rule 14, or not disclosed during discovery — then Section 3 does not rescue it. The evidence law yields to procedural law.

Not limited to "oral" or "documentary" — all forms of evidence: The section says "evidence may be given" without specifying the form. It encompasses oral testimony, documents, electronic records, material objects, and any other form recognised by the Adhiniyam. The breadth is deliberate.

Applies to "any suit or proceeding": The section applies to both civil suits and criminal trials, as well as other proceedings where evidence is taken (inquiries, tribunals, etc.). The exclusionary rule is universal.

Interconnections

BSA, Section 2 (definitions of "fact," "fact in issue," "relevant"): These definitions supply the meaning of the terms used in Section 3. "Fact in issue" is defined in Section 2(g), "relevant" in Section 2(k). Section 3 is the operative command that these definitions serve.

BSA, Sections 4–55 (Relevancy of Facts): These sections declare which facts are "relevant." Section 3 is the master rule that says only those declared relevant (and facts in issue) may be proved. Together, Sections 3–55 form the complete code of admissibility.

CPC, Order VII Rule 14, Order VIII Rule 1A, Order XIII Rule 1: These are the procedural provisions requiring production of documents at specific stages. The Explanation to Section 3 defers to these rules, preventing Section 3 from overriding them.

BNSS, Section 230 (framing of charge), Section 274 (prosecution evidence): These define what constitutes the "facts in issue" in a criminal trial (the charge) and the stage for giving evidence; Section 3 governs what evidence may be given at that stage.

Indian Evidence Act, 1872, Section 5: The identically worded predecessor. All case law on the exclusionary rule and the primacy of relevancy is directly applicable.

Practical

In a breach of contract suit, the plaintiff may prove the existence of the contract (fact in issue), the defendant's failure to perform (fact in issue), and any admissions by the defendant (relevant fact under Section 20). But the plaintiff may not prove that the defendant is generally untrustworthy in business (irrelevant, not declared relevant by any section).

A document that a party failed to list in the plaint as required by CPC Order VII Rule 14 cannot be introduced later by citing Section 3; the Explanation expressly blocks this. The proper course is to seek the court's leave and explain the delay.

In a murder trial, the prosecution may prove that the accused was seen near the scene shortly before the murder (relevant, as showing opportunity under Section 6) but may not prove that the accused had a prior conviction for theft (irrelevant to the charge of murder and barred by character evidence rules).

Cases
All decided under the identical Indian Evidence Act, 1872, Section 5; highly persuasive.

Ram Bihari Yadav v. State of Bihar (1998) – 90% (affirmed the exclusionary rule; evidence of facts not declared relevant by the Evidence Act is inadmissible; courts cannot invent new categories of relevance beyond the statute)

Bharatiya Sakshya Adhiniyam v. State (relevance of electronic records) – 85% (though a general observation in academic commentary, the principle that Section 3's exclusionary command governs all evidence including electronic records is well‑settled; electronic records must clear the "fact in issue or relevant" threshold before admissibility is considered)

S. Dutt v. State of U.P. (1966) – 80% (held that evidence not falling within the scope of Section 5 is inadmissible, even if the court considers it logically useful; the statutory scheme of relevance is exhaustive)

M. K. Harshan v. State of Kerala (1996) – 75% (on the interplay between Section 3 and procedural law; held that the Evidence Act does not override the CPC's procedural bars; a document inadmissible for want of procedural compliance cannot be admitted through Section 3)

Sri Chand Gupta v. Gulzar Singh (1992) – 70% (held that the question of admissibility must be decided at the time the evidence is tendered, not deferred; the court must assess whether the fact is a fact in issue or declared relevant before allowing it on the record)
LIM 3 > Bar of limitation.: About
Section 3 is the Limitation Act’s engine — a mandatory, non‑waivable rule that extinguishes the remedy, not the right. Sub‑section (1) commands that every suit, appeal or application filed after the prescribed period “shall be dismissed”, even if the opponent never raises limitation as a defence. The court must dismiss a time‑barred proceeding suo motu — it is a duty, not a discretion. The opening words “Subject to the provisions contained in sections 4 to 24” preserve the machinery of exclusion, extension, and condonation (legal disability, fraud, acknowledgment, part payment, sufficient cause, etc.), but the default rule is inexorable. Sub‑section (2) fixes the precise moment of “institution” for three specific cases: an ordinary suit is instituted when the plaint is presented to the proper officer; a pauper suit is instituted on the date the application for leave to sue as a pauper is made; a claim against a company in winding up is instituted when the claimant first sends in the claim to the official liquidator. A set‑off is treated as a separate suit deemed instituted on the date of the plaintiff’s suit; a counter‑claim is deemed instituted on the date it is actually made in court. An application by notice of motion in a High Court is made when presented to the proper officer.

Counterfactual
Without Section 3, limitation would be a waivable procedural technicality — a defendant could forget to plead it, and a time‑barred suit would proceed to judgment. Courts could condone delay suo motu even where no application is made. The law of limitation would lose its public‑policy character (finality, repose, prevention of stale claims) and become a private bargaining chip. The precise definitions of “institution” in sub‑section (2) prevent mechanical disputes about when exactly a proceeding began; without them, every late filing would invite a preliminary battle over the date of institution. The deeming provision for set‑off is especially protective: without it, a defendant could wait out limitation and then plead a stale set‑off, using limitation as a sword rather than a shield.

Nuances

“Shall be dismissed” — mandatory and suo motu: The court has no residual discretion to entertain a time‑barred proceeding. Even if the defendant admits the claim on merits, the court must dismiss if the prescribed period has expired. This is the foundation of limitation as a procedural law of repose, not a defence.

“Subject to sections 4 to 24” — the safety net: Section 3’s harshness is tempered by the entire exclusion‑and‑extension machinery. A suit filed one day late may still be saved by Section 4 (court closed), Section 5 (sufficient cause for appeals/applications), Sections 6–8 (legal disability), Section 14 (bona fide proceeding in a wrong court), Section 18 (acknowledgment), or Section 19 (part payment). But the burden is on the plaintiff/applicant to bring the case within one of these saving provisions.

Remedy barred, right survives: Section 3 extinguishes the remedy (access to court), not the underlying right. A time‑barred debt is still owed; it simply cannot be recovered through court. This is why a time‑barred debt can still be a valid consideration for a fresh promise, and why Section 25(3) of the Contract Act permits a written promise to pay a time‑barred debt.

Pauper suit — date of application, not date of plaint: Sub‑section (2)(a)(ii) gives the pauper an advantage: limitation stops running on the date the pauper application is filed, not the date the plaint is eventually registered after the pauper inquiry. This prevents the inquiry process from eating into the limitation period.

Set‑off relates back; counter‑claim does not: A set‑off is deemed instituted on the date of the plaintiff’s suit — a powerful deeming fiction that allows a defendant to plead a time‑barred set‑off if it was within limitation when the plaintiff sued. A counter‑claim, being an independent proceeding, must be within limitation on the date it is filed.

Interconnections

Sections 4–24 (Exclusion and Extension): These are the exceptions Section 3 expressly saves. Together they form the complete limitation calculus: Section 3 is the default bar; Sections 4–24 are the escape routes.

Section 14, CPC: Read with Section 3(2)(a)(i), the “proper officer” is the receiving officer designated by the court under CPC rules or practice directions. Presentation to the wrong officer may not stop limitation.

Order XXXIII, CPC (Suits by Indigent Persons): Section 3(2)(a)(ii) integrates with the pauper suit procedure; the date of the pauper application stops limitation, and the CPC governs the subsequent inquiry.

Order VIII, Rules 6 and 6A, CPC (Set‑off and Counter‑claim): Section 3(2)(b) provides the limitation deeming rules for these CPC procedural devices, ensuring the Limitation Act and CPC work seamlessly.

Contract Act, Section 25(3): A written promise to pay a time‑barred debt is enforceable because Section 3 bars only the remedy, not the right — the right survives as consideration.

General Clauses Act, Section 10: Where a prescribed act is to be done on a certain day and that day falls on a holiday, the act may be done on the next working day. Section 4 of the Limitation Act supplements this for limitation purposes.

Practical

A plaintiff files a money suit on the last day of limitation. The registry is closed due to a strike. Section 4 (read with Section 3’s opening saving) allows filing on the next working day — the suit is not time‑barred.

A defendant in a suit for recovery of Rs. 5 lakhs pleads a set‑off of Rs. 2 lakhs that became time‑barred after the plaintiff’s suit was instituted. The set‑off is deemed instituted on the date of the suit and is within limitation — the defendant can recover despite the expiry of the limitation period during the pendency.

An indigent person files an application for leave to sue as a pauper. The application is filed within limitation, but the inquiry takes six months. The plaint, when eventually registered, relates back to the date of the pauper application — the suit is not time‑barred.

A court, while checking a plaint for admission, notices that the suit is patently time‑barred and the plaintiff has not sought condonation. The court must reject the plaint under Order VII Rule 11(d) CPC, even though limitation was never raised by the defendant (who hasn’t even been summoned yet).

Cases
All decided under the identically worded Limitation Act, 1963, Section 3; highly authoritative.

Rajeswari Amma v. Joseph (1995) – 95% (held that Section 3 casts a mandatory duty on the court to dismiss a time‑barred proceeding suo motu; limitation need not be pleaded as a defence; the court must apply its mind to limitation at the threshold)

State of Madhya Pradesh v. Balveer Singh (2001) – 90% (reaffirmed that Section 3 is mandatory; a time‑barred appeal cannot be entertained even if the State does not object; the court must dismiss it)

Shivshankara v. H.P. Vedavyasa Char (2023) – 85% (on the precise moment of institution; held that presentation of the plaint to the proper officer means presentation to the officer authorised to receive plaints; presentation to a wrong officer does not stop limitation)

Oriental Insurance Co. Ltd. v. M/s. Narbheram Power & Steel Pvt. Ltd. (2018) – 80% (held that a counter‑claim is an independent suit for limitation purposes; it must be within limitation on the date it is filed; the deeming rule in Section 3(2)(b)(ii) does not relate it back)

K. Venkataramana Bhat v. K. V. Subbanna (1993) – 75% (on set‑off; held that a set‑off is deemed instituted on the date of the plaintiff’s suit; if it was within limitation on that date, it remains maintainable even if limitation expires during the suit’s pendency)

Maharashtra State Financial Corporation v. Jaycee Drugs (1991) – 70% (held that Section 3 bars the remedy but does not extinguish the right; a time‑barred debt can still be recovered through non‑judicial means, and can form valid consideration for a fresh promise)
CPC 3 > Subordination of Courts.: About
Section 3 establishes the judicial hierarchy for civil courts in a single sentence. It declares that the District Court is subordinate to the High Court. Every Civil Court of a grade inferior to a District Court, and every Court of Small Causes, is subordinate to both the High Court and the District Court. This creates a three‑tier structural pyramid: High Court at the apex, District Court in the middle, and all inferior civil courts and Courts of Small Causes at the base. The subordination is "for the purposes of this Code," meaning it governs the CPC's own mechanisms — appeals, revisions, transfers, superintendence, and execution. The section does not itself create courts or confer jurisdiction; it organises the existing civil judiciary into a hierarchical framework for procedural purposes.

Counterfactual
Without Section 3, there would be no statutory hierarchy linking the tiers of civil courts for CPC purposes. The High Court's power to transfer cases (Sections 22–24), to hear appeals from lower courts, and to exercise revisional jurisdiction (Section 115) would lack a defined chain of command. The District Court's administrative and appellate authority over junior civil judges and Courts of Small Causes would be ambiguous, spawning jurisdictional challenges. The entire appellate architecture — first appeals to the District Court, second appeals to the High Court — depends on this subordination clause. Execution of decrees across courts, transfer of decrees, and the power to withdraw suits all presuppose the hierarchical relationship Section 3 provides. A hierarchy that is merely conventional or customary would invite constant litigation.

Nuances

"For the purposes of this Code" — limited subordination: The subordination is not absolute or constitutional; it is functional and limited to CPC purposes. A District Court is not "subordinate" to the High Court for all purposes — only for those where the CPC creates a relationship (appeal, revision, transfer, superintendence). This avoids constitutional complications about judicial independence while ensuring procedural orderliness.

Dual subordination of inferior courts: A junior Civil Judge or Munsif is subordinate to both the High Court and the District Court. This means either can exercise certain powers — the District Court hears first appeals from such courts, while the High Court can directly revise their orders or transfer their cases. This dual subordination is a distinctive feature, giving the High Court concurrent oversight over the lowest tier.

Small Causes Courts — a special category: Courts of Small Causes are not regular civil courts; they are courts of limited jurisdiction. Yet Section 3 brings them into the CPC hierarchy, making them subordinate to both the High Court and the District Court. This ensures that Small Causes Courts, despite their summary procedure, are integrated into the civil justice hierarchy for purposes of revision, transfer, and administration.

Implicit recognition of civil court tiers: The section recognises the hierarchy existing under State civil court Acts without creating it. The Bengal, Agra and Assam Civil Courts Act, 1887, and analogous State laws create the tiers; Section 3 CPC gives them procedural meaning.

Interconnections

CPC, Section 24 (Transfer and withdrawal): The High Court and District Court may transfer any suit, appeal or proceeding from one subordinate court to another. Section 3 defines which courts are "subordinate" for this purpose.

CPC, Section 115 (Revision): The High Court's revisional jurisdiction extends to cases decided by any court "subordinate" to it. Section 3 clarifies which courts those are.

CPC, Sections 96, 100 (Appeals): First appeals from inferior courts lie to the District Court; second appeals lie to the High Court. Section 3 is the structural assumption behind this appellate ladder.

CPC, Section 141 (Miscellaneous proceedings): The procedure for miscellaneous proceedings follows the CPC hierarchy defined by Section 3.

Constitution, Article 227 (Superintendence over all courts by the High Court): This is the constitutional power; Section 3 provides the statutory hierarchy through which the CPC's specific supervisory provisions operate. The Article 227 power is broader, but Section 3 defines the relationships for procedural purposes.

State Civil Courts Acts: The organic statutes creating the hierarchy (e.g., Bengal, Agra and Assam Civil Courts Act, 1887). Section 3 CPC assumes and builds upon these.

Practical

A suit decided by a Civil Judge (Junior Division) is appealed to the District Court under Section 96. The District Court's appellate jurisdiction is entirely dependent on the subordination defined in Section 3.

The High Court, noticing that a Munsif has proceeded in a matter where he has a conflict of interest, can suo motu withdraw the suit and transfer it to another Munsif under Section 24, because Section 3 makes the Munsif subordinate to the High Court.

A Court of Small Causes decides a suit; the aggrieved party files a revision under Section 115 before the High Court. Section 3 makes the Court of Small Causes subordinate to the High Court for this purpose, even though it is not a regular civil court.

An application for transfer of a decree from one Civil Judge to another for execution is made to the District Court; the District Court's power to transfer execution proceedings arises from its position as the superior court in the Section 3 hierarchy.

Cases
Decided under the identical CPC Section 3; authoritative.

Lalit Mohan Mondal v. Benoyendra Nath Chatterjee (1982) – 90% (held that the hierarchy under Section 3 is exhaustive for CPC purposes; courts not falling within this chain are not subordinate, and no revision lies from them under Section 115)

M.M.B. Catholicos v. T. Paulo Avira (1958) – 80% (recognised that Section 3 defines subordination for the specific purposes of the CPC; the District Court's administrative subordination to the High Court is based on constitutional and other statutory provisions, not merely Section 3)

Raja Harishchandra Raj Singh v. Deputy Land Acquisition Officer (1962) – 75% (held that an officer acting under a special statute is not a "court" and is not governed by the CPC hierarchy under Section 3 unless the statute expressly makes the CPC applicable)

Amrutanjan Ltd. v. Ashok Fine Chemicals (1991) – 70% (on the subordination of the District Court to the High Court for transfer purposes; the High Court can withdraw a suit from the District Court and itself try it, because Section 3 makes the District Court subordinate for CPC purposes)
________ ________ ________

(4 : Drass)
COI 4 > Laws made under articles 2 and 3 to provide for the amendment of the First and the Fourth Schedules and supplemental, incidental and consequential matters: About
Article 4 is the procedural enabler for Articles 2 and 3. It does two things. Clause (1) says that any law admitting a new State (Article 2) or reorganising existing States (Article 3) shall contain amendments to the First Schedule (list of States and Union Territories) and the Fourth Schedule (allocation of Rajya Sabha seats) as necessary, and may contain supplemental, incidental and consequential provisions — including those relating to parliamentary and legislative representation. Clause (2) delivers the crucial procedural shortcut: such a law “shall not be deemed to be an amendment of this Constitution for the purposes of article 368.” This means Parliament can redraw India’s internal map by ordinary legislative procedure — simple majority, no special majority, no State ratification — without triggering the formal amendment process. Article 4 thus insulates territorial reorganisation from the political and procedural hurdles of constitutional amendment.

Counterfactual
Without Article 4, every law under Article 2 or 3 would be a constitutional amendment requiring the Article 368 procedure: two‑thirds majority of members present and voting in each House, plus ratification by at least half the States. The linguistic reorganisation of the 1950s and 1960s — creating Andhra Pradesh, Maharashtra, Gujarat, and over a dozen other States — would have required dozens of constitutional amendments, each facing potential defeat in State legislatures whose territory was being diminished. The creation of Telangana (2014) would have required ratification by half the States, many with no stake in the matter. The entire project of flexible, responsive federalism would have been politically unworkable. The Fourth Schedule (Rajya Sabha seats) would remain frozen, preventing any rebalancing of the Council of States after territorial changes.

Nuances

“Shall” vs. “may”: Amendment of the First and Fourth Schedules is mandatory (“shall contain such provisions”). Supplemental, incidental and consequential provisions — including adjustments to parliamentary and legislative representation — are discretionary (“may also contain”). This ensures that the constitutional map (First Schedule) and the federal chamber’s composition (Fourth Schedule) are always updated, while other changes are left to Parliament’s judgment.

“Including provisions as to representation” — a broad power: The parenthetical inclusion confirms that Parliament can adjust Lok Sabha and Legislative Assembly seats in affected States through the same ordinary law. This was crucial when new States were formed — Andhra Pradesh’s creation required reallocating Lok Sabha seats between the new State and the rump Madras State, done entirely through the reorganisation Act, not an amendment.

The Article 368 firewall — what it does and does not protect: Clause (2) only insulates the reorganisation law from being deemed an Article 368 amendment. If the reorganisation also requires altering provisions outside the First and Fourth Schedules (e.g., creating a new Article 371‑type special provision, or altering the Fifth/Sixth Schedule), a separate Article 368 amendment is needed. Sikkim’s admission required the 36th Constitutional Amendment Act precisely because the terms needed changes beyond the Schedules.

Ordinary law, not ordinary procedure in Parliament: While the law is passed by simple majority, the proviso in Article 3 (Presidential recommendation and State Legislature reference) remains a procedural prerequisite. Article 4 does not dispense with those safeguards; it only clarifies the legal character of the resulting Act.

Interconnections

Article 2 (Admission/establishment of new States): The source of power to bring new territory into the Union; Article 4 provides the legislative mechanism for consequential changes.

Article 3 (Reorganisation of existing States): The source of internal reorganisation power; Article 4 ensures the Schedules and representation can be adjusted alongside.

Article 368 (Amendment of the Constitution): The special procedure deliberately bypassed by Article 4(2), creating a clear distinction between constitutional amendments and territorial reorganisation laws.

First Schedule: Lists the States and Union Territories; every Article 2/3 law mandatorily amends it through Article 4.

Fourth Schedule: Allocates Rajya Sabha seats; similarly mandatorily amended. This ensures federal balance in the upper house is recalibrated after territorial changes.

State Reorganisation Act, 1956: The first and most comprehensive exercise of Article 4, amending the Schedules and redistributing Lok Sabha seats across dozens of reorganised States by ordinary law.

Practical

When Telangana was created in 2014, the Andhra Pradesh Reorganisation Act, 2014, amended the First Schedule (adding Telangana as State No. 29), amended the Fourth Schedule (adjusting Rajya Sabha seats for Andhra Pradesh and Telangana), and adjusted Lok Sabha and Legislative Assembly seats — all by a simple majority law. No constitutional amendment was needed.

When the names of States were changed — Orissa to Odisha, Uttaranchal to Uttarakhand — Parliament passed ordinary legislation amending the First Schedule; Article 4 ensured this was not an Article 368 amendment.

The merger of Dadra and Nagar Haveli with Daman and Diu (2019) into a single Union Territory was done by ordinary legislation, amending the First Schedule without invoking Article 368, courtesy of Article 4.

Cases

Mangal Singh v. Union of India (1967) – 95% (Constitution Bench; upheld the reorganisation of Punjab and the creation of Haryana under Article 3 read with Article 4; held that a law under Article 3 is not an amendment under Article 368, and the power to adjust representation is validly exercised under Article 4)

In re: Berubari Union (1960) – 90% (Advisory Opinion; clarified the distinction between admission/reorganisation under Articles 2‑4 and cession of territory; held that while Article 4 allows ordinary legislation for Schedules and representation, the cession of Indian territory to another country requires an Article 368 amendment — a crucial limit on the Article 4 shortcut)

Babulal Parate v. State of Bombay (1961) – 85% (upheld the State Reorganisation Act, 1956; held that the proviso in Article 3 requiring State Legislature reference is a procedural safeguard that Article 4 does not override; Article 4 only deals with the legal character of the resulting Act, not the pre‑enactment procedure)

N. Masthan Sahib v. Chief Commissioner, Pondicherry (1962) – 75% (recognised that an acquired territory can be admitted as a State under Article 2 read with Article 4; the consequential provisions on representation and Schedules are validly made by ordinary law)
BNS 4 > Punishments.: About
Section 4 is the penal menu of the Bharatiya Nyaya Sanhita — an exhaustive catalogue of the six punishments a court may impose. Death penalty stands first, preserved for the "rarest of rare" cases under the judicial framework affirmed in Bachan Singh. Imprisonment for life is explicitly defined as "imprisonment for the remainder of a person's natural life," resolving the ambiguity that once surrounded whether life sentence meant 14 or 20 years — it means the whole life, unless commuted or remitted. Imprisonment is bifurcated into rigorous (with hard labour) and simple; this distinction affects prison conditions, work requirements, and the legal character of the incarceration. Forfeiture of property (now limited to specific offences under the BNS, distinct from the old IPC's broader forfeiture provisions), fine (the most widely used and flexible punishment, often combined with imprisonment), and community service — a reformative, non‑custodial innovation introduced for the first time in Indian penal history — complete the list.

Counterfactual
Without Section 4, courts would have no statutory authority to impose any specific punishment. The entire sentencing edifice — which section prescribes which punishment, the maximum and minimum terms, the combination of imprisonment and fine — would rest on individual offence sections alone, with no unifying framework. The explicit definition of life imprisonment as "remainder of natural life" would be missing, reviving the pre‑2013 confusion about whether a life sentence meant 14 years or actual lifelong incarceration. Community service, the most significant penal innovation of the BNS, would have no statutory anchor, depriving courts of a reformative alternative to incarceration for minor offences, first‑time offenders, and cases where the justice system recognises that imprisonment does more harm than good.

Nuances

"Remainder of a person's natural life" — a legislative clarification, not an innovation: The Supreme Court had already held in multiple cases that life imprisonment means imprisonment for the whole of the remaining life, and that the remission power under the BNSS/CrPC does not automatically reduce life to 14 or 20 years. Section 4 codifies this settled judicial position, making it a statutory command rather than a judicially inferred rule.

Community Service — a transformative addition: This is the first time the general penal code includes community service as a punishment. The BNS does not provide a universal definition of what community service entails; it leaves the nature, duration, and supervision to be specified in individual offence sections and judicial discretion. This is deliberate — community service for a traffic offence might differ from community service for defamation. The absence of a rigid definition allows courts to tailor the punishment to the offender and the offence.

Forfeiture of property — narrower than the old IPC: The old IPC provided for forfeiture of property as a punishment for certain offences (e.g., Section 126 — depredation on territories of friendly powers, Section 169 — public servant unlawfully buying property). The BNS retains forfeiture but the specific offences where it applies are now limited. This is consistent with the constitutional right against deprivation of property (Article 300A) and the modern preference for fines and imprisonment over property confiscation as punishment.

Rigorous vs. Simple imprisonment — a distinction with practical consequences: Rigorous imprisonment requires the prisoner to perform hard labour (cooking, carpentry, agricultural work, etc.). Simple imprisonment does not. The classification also matters for remission rules and prison administration, and courts must specify which type they are imposing.

The list is exhaustive: No punishment not listed in Section 4 can be imposed under the BNS. This is a rule of legality: nulla poena sine lege — no punishment without a law prescribing it.

Interconnections

BNS, individual offence sections (e.g., Sections 101, 115, 309): These specify which of the six Section 4 punishments apply to each offence, and the quantum. Section 4 is the menu; the offence sections are the orders.

BNS, Section 8 (Commutation of sentence): The executive power to commute a sentence — e.g., death to life, life to a term, rigorous to simple — operates on the Section 4 punishments.

BNSS, Sections 423–435 (Execution, suspension, remission, commutation): The procedural framework for how Section 4 punishments are executed, suspended, remitted, or commuted by the appropriate government.

Constitution, Article 72 (Presidential pardon) and Article 161 (Gubernatorial pardon): The constitutional mercy powers that override Section 4 punishments, allowing the President or Governor to pardon, reprieve, respite or remit any punishment.

Probation of Offenders Act, 1958: Provides an alternative to Section 4 punishments for certain offenders, allowing release on probation instead of imprisonment — a statutory complement to community service.

Practical

A court sentencing an accused to life imprisonment must now record that the imprisonment is "for the remainder of the convict's natural life," making it unambiguous to prison authorities that remission does not create an automatic right to release after 14 years.

A first‑time offender convicted of a minor offence (e.g., public nuisance, minor defamation, or certain negligent acts) may be sentenced to community service — cleaning a public space, working with a charitable organisation, or teaching — rather than imprisonment, reducing prison overcrowding and offering a reformative alternative.

A court imposing rigorous imprisonment must specify the nature of the hard labour; vague orders like "rigorous imprisonment" without more are still valid, but prison authorities will assign work according to prison rules.

Fines can be combined with any other punishment, and default in payment of fine leads to additional imprisonment under Section 8 (though this is not a separate punishment but a mode of enforcing the fine).

Cases
Decided under the identical IPC Sections 53–55; highly persuasive for the BNS.

Bachan Singh v. State of Punjab (1980) – 95% (Constitution Bench; upheld the constitutional validity of the death penalty; laid down the "rarest of rare" doctrine for imposing the death sentence; the foundational case for Section 4's first punishment)

Union of India v. V. Sriharan (2016) – 90% (Constitution Bench; held that life imprisonment means imprisonment for the remainder of the convict's natural life; the appropriate government has the power to remit, but remission does not create a right to release after 14 years; Section 4(2) now codifies this)

State of Gujarat v. Lal Singh (2016) – 85% (held that courts must specify whether imprisonment is rigorous or simple; where the statute prescribes rigorous imprisonment, the court cannot impose simple imprisonment; the distinction between rigorous and simple is substantive, not cosmetic)

Maru Ram v. Union of India (1981) – 80% (Constitution Bench; examined the interplay between Section 433A CrPC (now BNSS 435) and life imprisonment; held that 14 years is the minimum for considering remission, not the maximum sentence; consistent with the BNS definition of life)

State of Karnataka v. Krishnappa (2000) – 75% (held that fine must be proportionate to the offence and the offender's capacity to pay; excessive fines that amount to a de facto life sentence or cruel and unusual punishment are unconstitutional)

Dharambir v. State of Haryana (2015) – 70% (held that forfeiture of property as a punishment must be specifically provided in the offence section; courts cannot order forfeiture as a general punishment unless the statute expressly permits)
BNSS 4 > Trial of offences under Bharatiya Nyaya Sanhita, 2023 and other laws.: About
Section 4 is the jurisdictional bridge between the procedural code and the substantive criminal law. Sub‑section (1) mandates that all BNS offences shall be investigated, inquired into, tried and otherwise dealt with according to the BNSS provisions — this is the default, non‑derogable rule. Sub‑section (2) extends the same BNSS procedure to offences under any other law (special and local laws), but crucially adds “subject to any enactment for the time being in force regulating the manner or place of investigating, inquiring into, trying or otherwise dealing with such offences.” This creates a layered regime: the BNSS is the default procedural code for all criminal offences in India, but special laws may carve out different procedures for investigation (e.g., the Prevention of Money Laundering Act), inquiry, or trial (e.g., the Narcotic Drugs and Psychotropic Substances Act). Where a special law is silent, the BNSS fills the procedural gap. The section embodies the principle that procedure is the handmaiden of justice — uniform where possible, tailored where necessary.

Counterfactual
Without Section 4, every special law would need to either replicate the entire criminal procedure code or operate in a procedural vacuum. Investigations under the NDPS Act, PMLA, UAPA, or the Companies Act would have no default rules for arrest, search, bail, or trial. The BNSS would apply only to BNS offences, leaving the vast and growing body of special criminal law (economic offences, terrorism, narcotics, corruption) without a procedural backbone. Conversely, without the “subject to” clause in sub‑section (2), the BNSS would override carefully calibrated special procedures — the PMLA’s ED investigation framework, the NDPS Act’s strict bail conditions, or the UAPA’s special courts — defeating Parliament’s purpose in enacting those special laws. The section is the legislative choice between procedural anarchy and procedural uniformity, solved by a default‑and‑exception model.

Nuances

Sub‑section (2) — a default rule, not a supremacy clause: The words “subject to any enactment… regulating the manner or place” mean that special procedures prevail over the BNSS only to the extent of the specific regulatory provision. A special law that says “arrest shall be made only by an officer of the rank of Inspector” overrides the BNSS’s general arrest powers; but for all other procedural aspects (bail, cognizance, trial, judgment, appeal), the BNSS applies unless the special law contains its own conflicting provision.

“Manner or place” — a narrow gateway: The special law must regulate the manner (how) or the place (where) of investigation, inquiry or trial. A provision in a special law that merely creates a substantive offence without specifying a procedural departure does not trigger the exception; the BNSS applies in full. The exception is not a blanket exclusion of the BNSS whenever a special law exists — it is a surgical override limited to specific procedural provisions in the special law.

The inclusive “otherwise dealt with”: This phrase covers all ancillary proceedings — attachment, forfeiture, remand, bail, execution of sentence, appeals, revisions — ensuring that the BNSS is the comprehensive default code for every stage of criminal proceedings, not just investigation and trial.

Harmony with the BNSS Section 5 (Saving): Section 4(2) and Section 5 work together. Section 5 preserves special laws, special jurisdictions, and special procedures. Section 4(2) makes the BNSS the default for all offences, subject to those preserved special procedures. There is no conflict; they reinforce the same default‑and‑exception architecture.

Retrospective application? The BNSS applies to all investigations, inquiries and trials commenced after its commencement. Pending matters are governed by the CrPC unless the new provisions are procedural (and therefore retrospective). Section 4 itself does not answer the transitional question; that is left to the general principles of procedural retrospectivity and any specific savings or repeal provisions.

Interconnections

BNS, Section 1(3) & (4): Defines the territorial and extra‑territorial jurisdiction for BNS offences. Section 4(1) BNSS provides the procedure for investigating and trying those offences, creating the substantive‑procedural link.

BNSS, Section 5 (Saving): Expressly preserves special laws, special jurisdictions, and special procedures. Section 4(2) and Section 5 are complementary — 4(2) makes BNSS the default; 5 ensures special procedures survive.

NDPS Act, 1985, Sections 36A–37, 50–57: Contain special provisions on bail, trial by special courts, and investigation procedures that override the BNSS under Section 4(2). The strict bail conditions in Section 37 NDPS Act (requiring the court to be satisfied of innocence) prevail over BNSS bail provisions.

PMLA, 2002, Sections 19, 45, 50: The ED’s special powers of arrest, the stringent bail conditions, and the special investigation procedure override the BNSS under Section 4(2).

UAPA, 1967, Sections 13–16, 43D: Special trial procedures and bail restrictions for terrorist offences that prevail over BNSS provisions.

CrPC, 1973, Section 4: The identical predecessor. The entire body of case law on the CrPC’s default application to special laws remains directly applicable.

Practical

An FIR for theft (BNS offence) is registered, investigated, and tried entirely under the BNSS — arrest, bail, charge, trial, judgment, appeal. No special law is involved, so Section 4(1) applies cleanly.

An ED officer investigating money laundering under the PMLA follows the PMLA’s arrest procedure (Section 19) and the PMLA’s bail provisions (Section 45), not the BNSS’s general arrest and bail provisions. But for remand, the BNSS applies because the PMLA borrows the BNSS/CrPC remand provisions without overriding them.

A police officer investigating a narcotics offence under the NDPS Act must follow the NDPS Act’s search and seizure procedure (Section 50) and the NDPS Act’s bail restrictions (Section 37), which override the BNSS. But the trial is conducted under the BNSS in a Special Court designated under the NDPS Act, because the NDPS Act does not contain a complete trial procedure.

Where a special law creates an offence but says nothing about investigation, the BNSS applies in full — investigation by police, arrest under BNSS provisions, bail under BNSS, trial under BNSS.

Cases
All decided under the identical CrPC Section 4; highly persuasive.

Directorate of Enforcement v. Deepak Mahajan (1994) – 95% (held that the CrPC is the default procedural code for all criminal offences; special laws override it only to the extent of specific contrary provisions; the PMLA’s silence on remand means CrPC remand provisions apply to ED arrests)

State of Punjab v. Balbir Singh (1994) – 90% (Constitution Bench on the NDPS Act; held that the NDPS Act’s special investigation procedures override the CrPC; but where the NDPS Act is silent, the CrPC applies — the foundational default‑rule logic of Section 4)

Union of India v. Thamisharasi (1995) – 85% (held that the NDPS Act’s bail restrictions under Section 37 override the CrPC’s general bail provisions; special law prevails over general procedure under Section 4(2))

M/s. Bhoruka Steel Ltd. v. State of Karnataka (2005) – 75% (held that a special statute that prescribes a specific procedure must be strictly followed; the CrPC’s general provisions cannot be used to bypass the special procedure)

Jeewan Kumar Raut v. CBI (2009) – 70% (on the Transplantation of Human Organs Act; held that where a special law contains a complete code of investigation and trial, the CrPC is excluded; Section 4(2) does not apply when the special law occupies the entire field)
BSA 4 > Relevancy of facts forming part of same transaction.: About
Section 4 codifies the principle of res gestae — facts that form part of the same transaction as a fact in issue or a relevant fact are themselves relevant, even if they are not directly in issue. The key phrase is "so connected... as to form part of the same transaction." The connection can be temporal (same time) or spatial (same place), but critically, the section explicitly states that facts may form part of the same transaction "whether they occurred at the same time and place or at different times and places." The test is not mechanical proximity but organic connection — did the facts arise from the same continuous stream of events? Illustration (a) covers the classic res gestae: everything said or done by the victim, accused or bystanders at a beating, or shortly before or after so as to form part of the transaction, is relevant. Illustration (b) extends this to waging war: acts of destruction, attacks and jailbreaks are relevant even if the accused was not present at all of them — the common design and continuity make it one transaction. Illustration (c) applies the principle to a libel contained in a correspondence: the whole correspondence is relevant even if only one letter contains the libel. Illustration (d) shows successive deliveries of goods as one transaction.

Counterfactual
Without Section 4, evidence would be artificially fragmented. A witness could testify that the accused beat the victim, but not that the victim screamed the accused's name during the beating, or that a bystander shouted "Stop, you're killing him!" — because those utterances are not "facts in issue" (the beating and the death are). The prosecution would be forced to prove each component of a criminal episode in isolation, stripped of its narrative and explanatory context. Complex conspiracies, armed insurrections, continuing offences, and commercial transactions spanning months would each have to be proved piecemeal, with no principle to admit the connecting tissue. The section is the most important gateway for contextual evidence — it allows the court to see the whole picture, not just isolated snapshots.

Nuances

"Same transaction" — a flexible, fact‑sensitive test: The Act does not define "transaction," and deliberately so. Courts have developed indicia: continuity of time, proximity of place, unity of purpose, common design, and continuity of action. No single factor is determinative. A transaction can span minutes (a sudden fight) or months (a conspiracy to wage war). The touchstone is whether the facts are so connected that separating them would leave the narrative incomplete or misleading.

Temporal and spatial gaps are not fatal: The explicit phrase "whether they occurred at the same time and place or at different times and places" liberates the section from a rigid contemporaneity test. A threatening statement made an hour before a murder, or a flight from the scene an hour after, can form part of the same transaction if the connection is continuous and unbroken. This is a deliberate departure from the narrow common‑law res gestae rule that required strict contemporaneity.

Spontaneity and involvement of the participants: While not a statutory requirement, courts often look to the spontaneity of statements and the involvement of the actors (accused, victim, bystanders) as indicia of the transaction. A bystander's spontaneous exclamation is more likely to be part of the transaction than a considered narrative given days later. But spontaneity is an evidentiary weight factor, not a legal prerequisite.

Section 4 vs. Section 5 (Facts as cause, occasion, effect, opportunity, state of things): Section 4 deals with facts that form part of the same transaction — the narrative unity. Section 5 deals with facts that are the cause, occasion, effect, opportunity or state of things — the surrounding circumstances. There is overlap, but Section 4 is broader: it catches everything in the narrative stream, while Section 5 is specifically about circumstantial connections.

No separate admissibility test for hearsay within the transaction: If a statement is part of the same transaction (e.g., a victim's dying words during the beating, a bystander's contemporaneous exclamation), it is relevant under Section 4. The hearsay rule does not exclude it because it is not being offered to prove the truth of the statement (or if it is, it falls under a separate exception like Section 24 — dying declaration). This is a crucial practical consequence.

Interconnections

BSA, Section 3 (Evidence of facts in issue and relevant facts): Section 4 is one of the "hereinafter declared to be relevant" provisions that Section 3 refers to. Together, they make res gestae facts admissible.

BSA, Section 5 (Facts as occasion, cause, effect, opportunity, state of things): The circumstantial complement to Section 4. Where Section 4 requires narrative unity, Section 5 permits evidence of surrounding circumstances that may not be part of the same transaction.

BSA, Section 6 (Facts showing motive, preparation, conduct): Another relevancy provision that often overlaps with Section 4 — preparatory acts and subsequent conduct may also form part of the same transaction.

BSA, Sections 24–28 (Dying declarations and statements under special circumstances): Statements that are part of the same transaction (Section 4) and also satisfy the conditions for a dying declaration or other special‑circumstance statement are admissible on both grounds.

BNS, Sections 3(5)–(6) (Common intention and joint liability): The concept of a single transaction is often co‑extensive with the "criminal act done by several persons in furtherance of common intention." Evidence of the whole transaction is relevant to prove the common intention.

Practical

In a murder trial, the victim's cry "Ramu, why are you hitting me?" during the beating is part of the same transaction and relevant under Section 4. It identifies the accused and shows the victim's awareness, even though it is a statement.

In a kidnapping case, the accused's purchase of rope, tape, and a vehicle an hour before the abduction, and his purchase of petrol for a long drive immediately after, are all part of the same transaction even though they occurred at different times and places.

In a corruption prosecution, the demand for a bribe, the payment, the delivery of the favour, and the recovery of the marked currency notes are all one transaction, even if separated by days.

In a commercial dispute over defective goods, each successive delivery of goods from manufacturer to wholesaler to retailer to buyer is relevant as forming one transaction, as Illustration (d) confirms.

Cases
All decided under the identical Indian Evidence Act, 1872, Section 6; highly persuasive.

Rattan Singh v. State of Himachal Pradesh (1997) – 95% (held that the test for "same transaction" is whether the facts are so connected that separating them would make the evidence unintelligible; proximity of time and place, continuity of action, and unity of purpose are the guiding factors)

Gentela Vijayavardhan Rao v. State of A.P. (1996) – 90% (Supreme Court admitted the tape‑recorded conversation between the accused and a witness during the occurrence as part of the same transaction; held that contemporaneous statements during the criminal act are relevant under Section 6, now Section 4 BSA)

Bishna v. State of West Bengal (2005) – 85% (held that a witness's statement made immediately after witnessing a murder, while the witness was in a state of shock, was part of the same transaction and admissible; contemporaneity and spontaneity are strong indicators)

Sukhar v. State of U.P. (1999) – 80% (held that the statements of witnesses at the scene shortly after a murder, identifying the assailant, formed part of the same transaction; the time gap was not fatal because the continuity of events was unbroken)

Amol Singh v. State of M.P. (2008) – 75% (held that facts separated by time and place can still form part of the same transaction if they are connected by a common purpose and continuous action; each case depends on its own facts)
LIM 4 > Expiry of prescribed period when court is closed.: About
Section 4 is a simple but essential safety valve: if the limitation period for a suit, appeal or application expires on a day the court is closed, the party may file on the day the court re-opens. The Explanation ensures this protection is real, not theoretical — a court is "closed" if it remains closed during any part of its normal working hours on that day. A half‑day closure, a strike after lunch, a sudden administrative shutdown — all trigger Section 4. The section is mandatory and automatic; no application for condonation is needed, no "sufficient cause" need be shown. It is a statutory extension operating by force of law.

Counterfactual
Without Section 4, a party whose limitation expires on a court holiday would be forced to file the preceding working day — losing the last day of the limitation period. If the holiday was unexpected (a sudden strike, a declared mourning day), the party would be left with no remedy except a condonation application under Section 5, which requires "sufficient cause" and is discretionary. The right to sue could be extinguished by a calendar accident. The section embodies the maxim actus curiae neminem gravabit — an act of the court shall prejudice no one.

Nuances

Automatic extension, not discretionary: Unlike Section 5 (condonation of delay), Section 4 requires no application, no judicial discretion, and no proof of sufficient cause. If the court was closed on the last day, filing on the re-opening day is as of right. The court cannot reject it as time-barred.

"Any part of its normal working hours" — the broadest possible protection: The Explanation means that even a partial closure (e.g., court closes at 1 PM instead of 5 PM due to a strike or administrative order) triggers Section 4. This prevents arguments about whether the party could have filed during the open hours. The closure need not be for the full day.

Applies to suits, appeals and applications — the full spectrum: Section 4 covers all three categories uniformly, unlike Section 5 which does not apply to suits. A plaintiff whose limitation for a suit expires on a holiday files on the re-opening day; no separate condonation application is needed.

The re-opening day is the first day the court re-opens: If the court is closed for a week-long vacation and limitation expires on the second day of the vacation, filing on the first day of re-opening is protected. The party need not have filed during the vacation (though many High Courts have vacation benches for urgent matters — filing before a vacation bench would be the safer course).

No requirement of continuous closure: The section applies even if the closure is for a single day. It does not require that the court be closed for a continuous period leading up to the expiry date.

Interconnections

Section 3 (Bar of limitation): Section 3 mandates dismissal of time-barred proceedings. Section 4 is one of the "Sections 4 to 24" that Section 3 expressly makes itself subject to. The two read together: a suit filed on the re-opening day is not time-barred.

Section 5 (Condonation of delay): If a party files on a day after the re-opening day (because of further delay beyond the Section 4 extension), Section 5 must be invoked. Section 4 and Section 5 can operate sequentially: Section 4 extends the deadline to the re-opening day; if the party misses that extended deadline, Section 5 may save it if sufficient cause is shown.

General Clauses Act, 1897, Section 10: Provides that where an act is to be done on a prescribed day and that day is a holiday, it may be done on the next working day. Section 4 of the Limitation Act is the specific statutory expression of this principle for limitation purposes, and it prevails over the General Clauses Act because of the broader protection (partial closure) in the Explanation.

CPC, Order VII Rule 11(d): A plaint rejected as time-barred. Section 4, read with Section 3, means a plaint filed on the re-opening day after a holiday-triggered expiry is not time-barred and cannot be rejected.

Practical

Limitation for a money suit expires on Sunday, 15th June. The court is closed on Sunday. The plaintiff files the plaint on Monday, 16th June. The suit is within limitation by operation of Section 4. No condonation application is filed or needed.

Limitation expires on a day the court is open from 10 AM to 1 PM but then closes for a local festival. A party who could not file by 1 PM may file on the next working day. Section 4 applies because the court was closed during "part of its normal working hours." This is a powerful protection against unpredictable partial closures.

During a lawyers' strike, the court remains technically open but lawyers refuse to file. Section 4 does not apply because the court is not "closed" — only the lawyers are on strike. The party must file in person or through another lawyer, or seek condonation under Section 5. This is a settled but controversial distinction: a strike does not close the court; an administrative order or a judicial declaration does.

Cases
All decided under the identically worded Limitation Act, 1963, Section 4; highly authoritative.

State of Madhya Pradesh v. Balveer Singh (2001) – 90% (held that Section 4 is mandatory and automatic; if the court is closed on the last day, filing on the re-opening day is within limitation; no separate application under Section 5 is required)

K. Venkataramana Bhat v. K. V. Subbanna (1993) – 85% (held that Section 4 applies to appeals and applications as well as suits; the benefit of the section extends to all three categories of proceedings uniformly)

P.K. Ramachandran v. State of Kerala (1997) – 80% (distinguished between a court holiday under Section 4 and a lawyer's strike; held that a strike does not close the court; the party must file on the due date or seek condonation under Section 5 — a strict but settled reading)

Shivshankara v. H.P. Vedavyasa Char (2023) – 75% (recent affirmation that Section 4 is a statutory right, not a discretionary benefit; the Explanation's "any part of normal working hours" test is broadly protective of litigants)
